Preface
This thesis concludes the authors master program in Sustainable Energy at the
Department of Mechanical Engineering, Section of Thermal Energy at Techni-
cal University of Denmark, DTU. The thesis was performed under the super-
vision of main supervisor Associate Professor Masoud Rokni and co-supervisor
Senior Researcher Ulrik Birk Henriksen.
I would like to thank Masoud for his guidance, supervision and our almost
weekly meetings, Ulrik and Rasmus from DTU Risø for invaluable help regard-
ing the gasiﬁcation technology. I would like to thank Arne Jørgensen Egelund
from DTU for moral support, discussion and guidance.
- Andreas W. L. Borgen 2016
iii
==PAGE:8==
==PAGE:9==
Abstract
Municipal solid waste can be considered a valuable resource to be used for
energy production. An decentralized small scale alternative to the conventional
energy recovery methods has been suggested. With a gasiﬁer, a heat treatment
of solid material, a combustible gas can be produced and fed to a solid oxide
fuel cell to produce power. The by-product heat from the processes can be
utilized to produce cooling through an absorption chiller plant.
The system has been designed and modelled with the in-house software DNA.
Simulations results in a thermal and exergetic eﬃciency of 35 and 32.8 %
respectively, a signiﬁcant increase in performance compared to the conventional
waste-to-energy methods. A parametric study has further improved the system
to 37.4 % thermal eﬃciency if better components is utilized. The absorption
plant can adequately utilize the by-product heat to provide chilled water for
district cooling with a 0.7 coeﬃcient of performance.
Diﬀerent municipal solid waste types has been analysed and evaluated based on
waste fractions, showing a signiﬁcant variance in properties depending on the
activity generating the waste. The diﬀerence in heating value between fuels
is over 30 % and the impact on the system performance shows that a good
estimate of waste composition is important for the design.
A thermoeconomic analysis shows that under given assumptions the system is
quite expensive, primarily due to the fuel cell and gasiﬁer component costs.
The resulting power production cost of 0.57 $/kWh is signiﬁcantly higher than
other power production methods, indicating that currently the fuel cell and
gasiﬁer technologies requires additional research and development to decrease
costs. The exergy analysis ﬁnds the high temperature diﬀerences and chemical
reactors in the system to be a major factor in energy ineﬃciencies.

Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
Shift in the energy sector
Denmark has in the recent decades been investing in a green transition of the
energy sector. From early 1970 Denmark has moved from being completely
dependent on imported oil to a power sector utilizing primarily coal. With
the more recent focus on fossil fuel depletion and the environmental impact of
coal power production, another shift has been happening in the Danish energy
systems. The current ambitious energy and climate policy of the European
Union and the Danish government, requires a more sustainable energy system
without fossil fuels[1].
Wind power plays a signiﬁcant part in this transition, together with biomass
as a substitute for coal in large power plants. With the introduction of sus-
tainable energy, such as wind and solar, into the Danish energy system, the
old model of large combined heat and power plants is no longer feasible to
the same extent. Two overarching energy system scenarios for Denmark are
considered by the Danish Energy Agency, a wind or a biomass based system.
Each scenario includes some biomass and wind, but one of the two technologies
are the focus, such that the wind scenario relies on electrical vehicles in the
transportation sector, while the biomass utilizes liquid biomass derived fuels.
As a consequence the biomass scenario is heavily reliant on importing biomass
as fuel both in power plants and for conversion to liquid fuels. The wind sce-
nario on the other hand can have problems with security of supply due to the
availability of renewable energy sources, but relies only on biomass which can
be produced locally[2].
No matter the scenario, more eﬃcient and sustainable energy production meth-
ods and technologies are needed in the future, if Denmark is to reach the goal of
a fossil fuel free society by 2050. The available fuel in both scenarios, primarily
biomass, must be utilized to the fullest potential.
Polygeneration
Cogeneration means the production of heat and power simultaneously, widely
accepted for having a higher energy eﬃciency and reducing primary energy
1
==PAGE:18==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
consumption compared to producing the two products in separate processes.
The beneﬁt of the concept is illustrated on ﬁgure 1.1 for separate production
and ﬁgure 1.2 for cogeneration with estimated products based on common
eﬃciencies.
System A
Power 33 MW
Fuel 88 MW
System B
Heat 52 MW
Fuel 72 MW
Figure 1.1: Illustrative example of separate production of heat and power.
System C
Power 33 MW
Heat 52 MW
Fuel 100 MW
Figure 1.2: Illustrative example of cogeneration of heat and power.
Polygeneration, or trigeneration, is the production of three or more products
in the same plant, further adding to the advantages of cogeneration, being
even more eﬃcient and less polluting.
The system consist of at least one
power producing unit and one or more heat recovery systems, producing district
heating/cooling, process steam, liquid fuels and so forth. Utilizing the waste
heat from power production is a key factor in an energy eﬀective system, in
particular smaller systems closer to potential consumers[3].
Decentralization of small scale cogeneration plants is widespread in Denmark,
but does not extend to waste incineration, which is far more centralized[4][5].
Many power stations in Denmark has surplus heat available all year long and
utilizes it for district heating during the winter months, but during the summer
most of this available heat is dispersed in the atmosphere. This is especially the
case for waste incineration plants, which has to operate all year to dispose of
municipal waste. The temperature of district heating is however conveniently
adequate to utilize in absorption chiller plants, producing district cooling from
the available surplus heat[6].
Waste as a valuable resource
According to The World Bank’s report What a waste: a global review of solid
waste management from 2012, municipal solid waste (MSW) generation will
continue to increase in the coming decades. From an estimate of 1.3 billion
tonnes MSW in 2013 to 2.2 billion tonnes in 2025, primarily due to population
growth combined with urbanization. Furthermore the authors, Hoornweg et
al. predicts that MSW generation will not peak in the current century[7].
Solid waste generation varies signiﬁcantly depending on the region in the world,
income level, degree of urbanization and so forth. The income level in particular
==PAGE:19==
1.1. MOTIVATION
3
is important, as low income capita generate between 0.6 and 1.0 kg MSW per
day, while high income capita generate from 1.1 to 4.5 kg/day.
The waste
generated by the urban population in four income categories are shown in
ﬁgure 1.3. The ﬁgure displays how the growth in waste generation is expected
to increase more than twofold for the lower and middle lower income countries,
while the growth is more moderate in the higher income countries. The high
income countries (such as Denmark) are responsible for the largest generation
rate of MSW in 2010, but on the other hand have a far superior waste handling
system, compared to the developing world[8].

In the future scenarios mentioned previously, MSW is considered a biomass
feedstock and valuable resource. In Denmark waste is sorted, so that only the
fraction which can not be reused or recycled is used as fuel in waste incineration
plants.
These plants however suﬀer from rather poor energy eﬃciency and
energy utilization[9].
Novel technologies
To more eﬃciently utilize the MSW as a fuel, new technologies must be consid-
ered to replace or support the current. The main problem with MSW as a fuel
is the heterogeneity. MSW is comprised of many diﬀerent diverse materials of
varying size and density with diﬀerent properties.
Gasiﬁcation is an alternative to the conventional waste incineration combustion
of solid waste, often utilized for more uniform biomass feedstock such as wheat
or wood pellets. Broadly speaking, gasiﬁcation is a high temperature thermal
conversion process of carbonaceous solid and liquid material into a combustible
gas. One advantage of gasiﬁcation compared to combustion is the relatively
clean gas, and the possibility of further removal of undesirable particles from
the gas before utilizing it in a power producing unit. Furthermore, a power
producing unit will not suﬀer the same technical limitations as a conventional
waste incineration plant in terms of corrosion issues when utilizing MSW gas
from gasiﬁcation[10].
The gas produced from the gasiﬁcation process can be used in conventional
==PAGE:20==
4
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
power production units, such as a gas turbine or boiler in a steam cycle. A
fairly new concept is to integrate the gasiﬁcation process with a fuel cell. Fuel
cells are an interesting small scale technology, which converts hydrogen and
carbon rich fuels into electricity without the need for a burner or conversion
through mechanical work.
Several diﬀerent fuel cell designs in various stages of development are available
today. The solid oxide fuel cells (SOFC) in particular are interesting from a
small scale system point of view. The SOFC has two major features making it
excellent for gas produced from MSW. A high operating temperature (700 to
1100 ◦C) allows the fuel cell to be insensitive towards the gas composition and
simpliﬁes the design. Furthermore, the high temperature waste heat produced
during the chemical conversion enables the SOFC to work as a co- or trigener-
ation system[11].

Overview of main processes for waste to energy conversion
through gasiﬁcation and SOFC with bottoming cycle heat recovery.
Figure 1.4 shows the system concept diagram of a gasiﬁcation integrated SOFC
with heat recovery. Apart from MSW fuel, the system only utilizes air and
steam which is produced in the process, to produce electricity, heating and
cooling.
1.2
Objectives
The objective of this thesis is to investigate the viability of the system described
in ﬁgure 1.4, a small scale gasiﬁcation integrated SOFC system for trigeneration
of electricity, heating and cooling.
• How should a system combining a gasiﬁer, SOFC and absorption plant
be designed?
• What eﬀect does the MSW composition have on the operation of the in-
vestigated system?
• What is the cost of said system, is it viable today or in the future?
To examine these questions, a thermodynamic model and following simulations
will be carried out in the in-house DNA1 software at the Section of Thermal
1Further introduced in section 4.1.
==PAGE:21==
1.2. OBJECTIVES
5
Energy in the Department of Mechanical Engineering at DTU. The system will
be evaluated based on thermodynamic principles of energy and exergy analysis
as well as economic estimates and considerations.
==PAGE:22==
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 2
Background and Technologies
This chapter will lay the foundation for further analysis of the studied system
in the following chapters. The background for the project will be presented
along with the relevant technologies included in the system. The project relies
primarily on the Danish system and Danish data to the extend where it is
available and applicable.
2.1
Future Danish energy system
Before going into the details of technologies, methods and background informa-
tion for the project, a brief overview of the current and future Danish energy
system is in order. As mentioned in the introduction both Denmark and the
EU as a whole are in at the moment attempting to reduce emissions and thus
the impact of human activities on the climate.
For Denmark this results in a future energy system focused on renewable en-
ergy, primarily wind power and to some extend solar, with biomass conversion
as a sustainable substitute for liquid fuels. In the recent past, Denmark has
been almost entirely based on coal power for base load production with gas
turbines mostly used in peak consumption.
However, most of these power
plants should in the coming 35 years be taken out of commission and either
replaced with wind power or reconﬁgured to utilize biomass. This transition is
not without challenges, as a energy system based on renewable energy has to
be either vast in a geographical terms to cover weather variations or intercon-
nected with neighbouring regions with other types of renewable energy sources,
such as hydro power in the other Scandinavian countries or nuclear power in
Germany1. The current transmission lines will likely not suﬃce in a scenario
where most of Denmark’s power consumption are produced with wind energy
and the wind is not blowing for an extended time[12].
As of 2012 the power consumption in Denmark was covered by 52 % fossil
fuels, 33 % wind power and 15 % other sustainable energy sources. The past
consumption divided among wind power, other sustainable energy and fossil
fuels can be seen on ﬁgure 2.1 together with the consumption in 2012 and po-
litical goal for 2020. It is not clear in which category waste incineration are
1Whether nuclear power is renewable, sustainable or otherwise is possibly up for debate,
together with the fact that Germany might now be moving away from nuclear power.
7

included[13]. The previous Danish government2 deﬁned energy policy mile-
stones for Denmark, aiming towards all heat and power production being cov-
ered by sustainable energy by 2035. By 2050 this should furthermore include
the transportation sector, a sector current based almost solely on liquid com-
bustible fuels today[14].

Figure 2.1: The power consumption of Denmark in year 2000 and 2012 with

Two issues with regard to introducing renewable energy in Denmark is ﬂexi-
bility and district heating. It is widely accepted that renewable energy sources
has problems with ﬂexibility and availability and requires large scale energy
storage options, which has yet to be solved. The other issue is that of district
heating production. Wind power has no surplus heat which can be used for
district heating production, as opposed to a CHP ﬁred with coal. Solar ther-
mal heating is said to be a potential large scale district heating production
candidate, but has several concerns regarding proﬁtability, corrosion etc.[15].
The current government (as of November 2015) recognizes the issue and are
considering electrically powered heat pumps a viable solution[13].
2.2
What is municipal solid waste
The deﬁnition of municipal solid waste diﬀers some depending on the source.
According to the world bank the OECD deﬁnes MSW as waste from house-
holds, commerce and trade, oﬃce buildings, institutions and small businesses.
Furthermore yard and garden waste is included in this deﬁnition, but sewage
waste, demolition and construction waste is not[8]. The OECD deﬁnition is
used throughout this project since sewage waste is not considered in any ex-
tend, due to the special treatment requirements, compared to MSW. Waste will
diﬀer substantially according to the producer. Household waste (HHW) will
have a diﬀerent composition from waste produced at a shopping center or hos-
pital. Sorting and recycling further impact the composition, both the degree
of recycling and if the sorting is conducted at the producer (in the household)
2As of writing this, it is unclear to what extend the current government will continue
towards the same goals.

or at the waste handling site prior to incineration.
Table 2.1 shows an overview of MSW handling in selected European countries.
For some countries the data used by Eurostat dates back to 2007 and 2008,
meaning the situation has liked changed partially since then[16]. Even con-

EU has a large potential for utilizing MSW as a fuel in energy production pur-
poses. Both Greece, Poland and Spain landﬁll over 50 % of produced MSW,
while the Nordic countries landﬁll close to zero percent. For countries without
an extensive district heating grid, local small scale plants close to the waste
producers could prove a viable solution. A system supplying power, heating
and cooling without the large investment cost of setting up a district heating
network, while additionally reducing emissions/pollution and the land used for
landﬁlls, could provide signiﬁcant improvements.

2.2.1
MSW in Denmark
For Denmark the MSW situation between 2011 and 2013 can be seen on ﬁgure
2.2. Primary waste being all received by the diﬀerent treatment stations, for
recycling this means all waste collected for recycling, of which some is transfer
to be incinerated or landﬁlled after sorting, normally classiﬁed as secondary
waste. As a consequence, both the share of incinerated and landﬁlled waste is
likely to be higher, correlating well with table 2.1 when also considering the
age diﬀerence in the data[17].
In previous years around 30 % of Danish MSW is incinerated with up to 70 % for
certain industries[17]. As of 2014 Denmark has 29 incineration plants located in
the vicinity of most major cities[5]. About two-thirds of the plants are combined
heat and power while the remaining is producing solely heat. The incineration
plants supply towards 20 % of Danish district heating consumption.

The remaining two percent for each year is temporary landﬁlls and special

MSW is normally sorted to some degree, depending on municipality, type of
housing and so forth. For household waste dry paper and board without food
scraps are collected separately together with glass, metal and electronic waste.
Furthermore, biodegradable waste is in rare cases separated from the remaining
household waste, which then consists of wet packaging, human hygiene waste
etc. Hard plastics are often in the form of bottles and are in Denmark in most
cases handed in via the bottle return scheme, meaning the plastics in MSW
are often thinner food packaging and plastic ﬁlm. A further sorting happens at
waste handling, either manual sorting on a conveyor belt or mechanic sorting.
2.2.2
Waste incineration
A waste incineration plant is designed only for the burning of MSW and will
normally not be utilized with other fuels since incinerating waste requires spe-
cial care. The main components are a reception area with feeding system, a
furnace, a boiler, a steam turbine cycle and a cleaning system. A sketch of the
system can be seen on ﬁgure 2.3. For all standard system conﬁgurations the
waste is fed to the furnace/incinerator where heat is released in a ﬂue gas and
utilized for the heating of water. In a CPH plant the water is superheated into
high pressure steam and utilized in a back-pressure steam turbine to produce
heat and power. Smaller waste incineration plants are in some cases designed
only to produce heat, foregoing the CHP plant entirely and heat exchanging
between the fuel gas and district heating system directly. After utilizing the
heat in the ﬂue gas an extensive cleaning system is required to remove dust,
heavy metals, acidic gasses and dioxins, resulting in both a price increase and
production of hazardous/toxic waste.

According to the Rambøll report[19] from 2006, one ton of MSW can produce
around 0.67 MWh electricity and about 2 MWh heat. The average electrical
eﬃciency of the system is somewhere around 20 % and have a typical CO2
emission factor of about 37.0 kg/GJ.

25 and 20 years respectively before major renovation of the plants. Figure take

In recent years the waste incineration plants (WtE3) in Denmark have had more
capacity available than the amount of waste generated, leading to a trend of
importing waste to incinerate. Even when considering transport of the waste,
this is a good practise seen both from an economic and environmental point of
view. Most of the waste is produced in the UK (and to some extent Ireland)
where they have severe issues with WtE capacity and thus end up with unde-
sirable landﬁlls if not for exporting. To a smaller degree waste is important
from Norway where utilizing the energy in combined heat and power plants

are less common as the district heating network in Norway is supplying only

2.3
Gasiﬁcation
This section will brieﬂy describe the gasiﬁcation concept, more in detail the
working principle and reactions, present several types of gasiﬁcation reactors
and systems and investigate gasiﬁcation with regard to handling of waste.
The deﬁnition of gasiﬁcation is the thermal conversion of organic material to
combustible gas. The transformation is performed by heating the biomass with
the presence of a gasiﬁcation agent. By controlling the agent ﬂow rate in the
gasiﬁcation process it is possible to perform a incomplete combustion4 of the
feedstock and convert solid heterogeneous material to a gaseous fuel usable for
energy production through conventional conversion technologies, such as a gas
turbine, gas engine or a steam boiler[21]. Perhaps the best known early example
of gasiﬁcation is in London, where it saw large scale use in the production of
"city-gas" from coal, as means of simpler distribution compared to delivering
coal around the city.
Compared to direct combustion of MSW, gasiﬁcation oﬀers some interesting
advantages. Gasiﬁcation plants are possible in smaller scale while still achieving
high eﬃciencies, where as direct combustion plants are too expensive due to the
steam boiler5 and the produced gas are easier to handle and control compared
to solid fuel MSW. By being able to clean the gas before combustion, the
issue with corrosive HCl in the boiler is removed, which would allow for a
higher degree of superheat and up to 6 % points improvement in electrical
eﬃciency[10]. The technology is however not without issues, the produced gas
being both toxic and explosive and plants utilizing gasiﬁcation tend to be more
complex, both the design and during operation[22].
The distinction between products from gasiﬁcation is not clear. Producer gas
is the gas coming out of the gasiﬁcation reactor, comprised of hydrogen H2,
carbon monoxide CO, carbon dioxide CO2, various hydrocarbons for example
4Less than suﬃcient air to reach stoichiometric combustion.
5A small scale plant could incinerate waste and not utilize a steam cycle, but the eﬃciency
are less than desirable compared to the potential.
==PAGE:29==
2.3. GASIFICATION
13
CH4 and impurities, such as tars and sulphur. Synthetic gas on the other hand
is a processed gas, cleaned of impurities (tars) and CO2, comprised of H2 and
CO[21]. This project use the term producer gas or product gas for any gas
produced via gasiﬁcation, as the SOFC does not require clean synthetic gas6.
2.3.1
Working principle
Gasiﬁcation is in general a complex process with a number of physical and
chemical interactions happening at high temperatures in several stages. Drying
and preheating takes place at up to 150 ◦C, while pyrolysis occurs at up to
600 ◦C depending on feedstock and reactor type. The ﬁnal stage, gasiﬁcation
(partial oxidation), temperature is dependant on the gasiﬁcation agent available
in the reactor.
The ﬁrst step in the gasiﬁcation process is a pre-heating and drying of the
feedstock, removing moisture from the fuel before further heating. By drying
the feedstock before entering the reactor, the heating value of the produced
gas will be higher as no energy is needed to evaporate the moisture inside the
reactor.
Dry material
Heat
Pyrolysis
Devolatilization 
Permanent gasses
Tarry vapors
Char
Figure 2.5: Input and output of the pyrolysis process (devolatilization).
The initial reactions happen during the pyrolysis process, ﬁgure 2.5, a pre-stage
to the actual gasiﬁcation in an inert atmosphere. Here the feedstock is heated
to upwards of 600 or 700 ◦C and the volatile material will start to decompose
and release light gasses and tars while the remaining solid will form char. Light
permanent gasses are mostly CO, CO2, H2 and light hydrocarbons. Tars are
one of the most problematic aspect of gasiﬁcation. Maniatis et al. deﬁnes tars
as ’hydrocarbons with molecular weight higher than benzene’ following a series of
meetings by industry experts in 1998 and 99[23]. Tars also include polyaromatic
hydrocarbons7 and oxygen-containing hydrocarbons, many which will condense
into a thick liquid at low temperature and can contain a signiﬁcant portion of
the feedstock heating value. At temperatures above 400 ◦C tars can solidify
into char. Both in solid and liquid form the tars will clog equipment and can
deactivate catalysts and sorbents used downstream in the gas cleaning. The
composition and concentration of tar depends both on the temperature, the
pressure and the residence time inside the reactor[24]. Char is porous solid
remains of the feedstock comprised of carbon and inorganic material[21].
The gasiﬁcation stage8 (oxidation) depends on the reactor type. For downdraft

8Sometimes referred to as the reduction zone.

gasiﬁers the process is a partial combustion of the light gasses and tars in the
pyrolysis product to produce heat to drive the process, while the combustion
in ﬂuidised bed includes all the pyrolysis products. Due to the lack of oxygen,
a full combustion is not possible, nor desirable. The solid char and the light
gasses react with oxygen steam and CO2 in the gas-solid reactions(eq. (2.1)
- eq. (2.4)) and gas-phase reactions (eq. (2.5) - eq. (2.6)). The reactions
involving tars are somewhat more complicated due to the complex composition
of tars, but in general tars will decompose to smaller and smaller hydrocarbons
in endothermic reactions at high temperature[22], .
The reactions will reach an equilibrium with the amount of oxygen available,
such that no free oxygen is present at the gasiﬁer outlet. If standard air is used
as the oxidant in the reactions, a signiﬁcant amount of the nitrogen present in
the air will pass through the gasiﬁer without noteworthy reactions taking place,
resulting in a dilution of the produced gas[21]. Other reactions take place, but
these are the main reactions to determine the gas composition at the gasiﬁer
reactor outlet. For more details on reactions, see the MSW gasiﬁcation review
by Arena from 2012 in [22].

heat necessary to drive the processes. The tar will to some extend decompose
into smaller hydrocarbons, but the remaining tars can cause issues with the
equipment in form of clogging. Pyrolysis and gasiﬁcation can happen in the
same reaction or separately in a two-stage reactor. The two-stage reactor has
several advantages with regard to producer gas quality and tar content, but
adds more complexity to the system. In particular the possibility of separating
the pyrolysis gas and oxidizing it before the gasiﬁcation process greatly reduces
tar content[25]. One- or two-stage gasiﬁcation should be a cost balance between
the added performance of the plant and the added equipment/control required.
The density of waste fractions in MSW vary quite signiﬁcantly and it is likely
that some kind of shredder is necessary to achieve a more homogeneous mix[26].
This process and pre-treatment stage is not considered and it is assumed that
the waste is prepared for the gasiﬁcation in a reasonable manner.
2.3.2
Gasiﬁer types
Gasiﬁers are classiﬁed by their heating method, direct or indirect. The indi-
rect method requires an external heat source and can be considered as a high
temperature pyrolysis reactor. Since no air is used (only steam), the gas will
not be diluted by nitrogen and thus able to reach signiﬁcantly higher heating
values. The direct method requires air for partial combustion of the feedstock
to generate heat, but no external heating is needed after start up. The direct
method of gasiﬁcation is the one investigated via literature, while indirect gasi-
ﬁcation is not considered in this project11. Due to the added complexity and a
lack of external heat source, indirect gasiﬁers are deemed unsuitable for local
small scale MSW gasiﬁcation operation.
Several types of gasiﬁcation reactors are available today. The main diﬀerences
are in which manner heat is supplied, the operating pressure, temperature
ranges and in what way the feedstock is introduced. Some of the most com-
mon gasiﬁers are presented below, but for a more expansive list see [22].
In a ﬁxed bed gasiﬁcation reactor the feedstock (woodchips, waste, etc.) is fed
from the top of the gasiﬁer. Depending on conﬁguration the gas extraction
takes place either at the top or at the bottom. In both cases the feedstock
will fall from the top and pass through the least hot part of the reactor, in
11One way of utilizing the indirect could be solar power
==PAGE:32==
16
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND AND TECHNOLOGIES
which drying takes place, followed by the pyrolysis. The char produced in the
pyrolysis will form a hot bed in lower part of the reactor, which will move up
or down depending on operation12. Depending on type, the air is introduced
at diﬀerent points, resulting in diﬀerent reduction zones, as seen on ﬁgure 2.7.
The updraft conﬁguration causes signiﬁcant tar concentration in the product
as the tar from the pyrolysis is carried upwards. The same is not the case in
the down draft, where the pyrolysis tar passes through the hottest part of the
reactor, resulting in superior thermal tar cracking[10][22].
Figure 2.7: Schematic working principle for the up- and downdraft gasiﬁers.
Taken from [10]. Reduction zone = gasiﬁcation zone, hearth zone = combustion
zone.
Fluidised bed gasiﬁers utilize an inert material such as sand to create a liquid-
like bed in the reactor via an up-ﬂowing gasiﬁcation agent. Depending on the
agent velocity two types of ﬂuidised bed conﬁgurations are possible, at low ve-
locities a bubbling bed13 while at high velocities the bed material circulates by
exiting the reactor with the gas at the top and are to be ﬁltered and returned
to the reactor, normally via a cyclone. The two conﬁgurations can be seen on
ﬁgure 2.8. There are no distinguishable reaction zones in ﬂuidised gasiﬁers and
all reactions takes place at a uniform temperature[10]. One of the advantages of
the ﬂuidised bed is the lower temperatures, reducing ash melting and sintering
while still having a reasonably high carbon conversion factor (close to 90 %,
compared to ﬁx bed which has 95 - 100%)[21]. For more details on gasiﬁcation
subtypes, please see literature [10, 21, 22].
12Often also called a moving bed gasiﬁer.
13Comparable to boiling liquid.
==PAGE:33==
2.3. GASIFICATION
17
Figure 2.8: Schematic working principle for the bubbling and circulating bed
gasiﬁers. Taken from [10].
2.3.3
Gas cleaning
Aravind et al. did an extensive review and analysis of high temperature gas
cleaning in conjunction with SOFC[24]. The syngas produced during gasiﬁ-
cation can contain multiple containments, such as tar, hydrogen sulﬁde H2S,
hydrogen chloride HCl and particulates.
The impact of tars on SOFC operation is not well documented, but could pos-
sibly lead to clogging and degradation of the fuel cell. A few ppms of tar should
however not have any eﬀect on the SOFC operation, but a fair amount of the
feedstock potential heating value can be embodied in the tar.
Two overall
options for tar-removal are non-catalytic and catalytic. By increasing the tem-
perature in the gasiﬁer it is possible to lower the concentration, but this will
also negatively impact the heating value of the gas. Another option is catalytic
removal, based on chemical reactions with the tars. This can either be done in
the gasiﬁcation reactor (with a catalytic bed material in BFB or CFB) or as
hot gas cleaning after the gas outlet.
Sulphur, both in form of H2S and COS, are in practise poison for the SOFC
and must be removed if not to cause irreversible damage. Even low ppm of
sulphur can decrease the performance of the fuel cell, by being absorbed in the
anode and consequently limiting fuel absorption. The investigation by Aravind
et al. concludes that down stream desulfurization is possible with zinc-based
materials, which can reduce the concentration to 1 ppm.
HCl is less troublesome than H2S, but can still cause issues with corrosion and
cause cell degradation. The preferred method of removal is like for sulphur a
down stream reactor with an absorbing material such as alkali or sodium, both
==PAGE:34==
18
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND AND TECHNOLOGIES
able to reduce HCl concentration substantially[24].
2.3.4
MSW Gasiﬁcation
The gasiﬁcation process oﬀers signiﬁcant advances in energy recovery and emis-
sion reduction as a thermal treatment of waste. The most important factor re-
garding gasiﬁcation of MSW is choosing the correct reactor, depending on the
waste characteristics, which in itself presents an issue due to the heterogeneity
of MSW[10]. The lack of operational experience with commercially gasifying
waste is another challenge, which the technology has to overcome[22].
A small-scale experiment utilizing a bubbling ﬂuidized bed gasiﬁer to produce
syngas was conducted by Arena et al. in 2010[27]. The experiment investigated
diﬀerent waste-derived fuels with a capacity up to 100 kg/h. Utilizing various
bed materials in the gasiﬁer, the naturally-occurring mineral olivine14 was iden-
tiﬁed as a strong candidate for commercial gasiﬁcation due to the bed materials
ability to reduce tar content15 and increase of hydrogen and carbon monoxide
in the synthetic gas. This conclusion is in agreement with Aravind[24], which
also consider olivine viable as a bed material in BFB gasiﬁers with regard to
waste gasiﬁcation.
Directly comparing the cost of gasiﬁcation to conventional WtE technology (a
new and an old technology) is not possible, since R&D costs are included in
the new, but not the old[10].
2.4
Fuel cells
Fuel cells (FC) convert chemical energy from reactions into electricity without
the presence of combustion. The fuel cell consists of two electrodes, an anode
and a cathode, with an electrolyte layer/membrane in between. A fuel is fed
to the anode side and an oxidant or simply air is fed to the cathode side. The
electrolyte only allow positive ions to move through from anode to cathode (and
opposite for negative ions) and works as an insulator for electrons. The free
electrons must recombine on the other side of the electrolyte based on starting
point, thus moving through the circuit and creating an electric circuit[11].
Apart from electricity the fuel also generates by-products in the form of water
and heat via the exothermic chemical reactions. Using petroleum fuels, fuel
cells are able to reach high eﬃciencies (above 45 %) as stand-alone compared to
conventional combustion technologies. The applications of fuel cells are many,
anything from stationary power stations to person transportation are possible.
With regard to waste for example, the methane produced in untreated landﬁlls
are sometimes utilized in fuel cells.
2.4.1
Development
Some controversy remains about the invention of the fuel cell, but the basic
principle was investigated by both Christian F. Schönbein and William Grove
in late 1838. A rivalry between the two scientists went on for some time, but it
14A neo-silicate of Fe and Mg.
15According to the author, the tar content was below detectable values for some fuels.
==PAGE:35==
2.4. FUEL CELLS
19
is not clear who ﬁrst came up with the original idea. The ﬁrst device utilized
two platinum electrodes connected to sealed containers of oxygen and hydrogen
while submerged in sulphuric acid to create a stable ﬂowing current.
It was not until the middle to late part of the 20th century before fuel cells saw
ﬁrst commercial use, after Thomas F. Bacon presented a working 5 kW alkaline
fuel cell, which later lead to NASA utilizing them for power generation aboard
the space shuttle orbiters. Since then development has focused on reducing
installation costs, increasing performance and prolonging lifetime[11][28].
2.4.2
Classiﬁcation
Fuel cells are classiﬁed based on the electrolyte type and fuels and grouped in
to either low or high operating temperature (50 to 250 ◦C and 650 to 1000 ◦C
respectively). The low temperature fuel cell types are Proton exchange mem-
brane fuel cell (PEMFC), Alkaline fuel cell (AFC) and Phosphoric acid fuel cell
(PAFC). The high temperature FCs are Molten carbonate fuel cell (MCFC),
Direct methanol fuel cell (DMFC) and Solid oxide fuel cell (SOFC).
Anode
Cathode
Electrolyte
Fuel inlet H2
Surplus fuel H2
Load
O2
H2O + O2
H+
H+
e-
e-
Figure 2.9: Working principle of a PEM fuel cell operated with hydrogen as
fuel.
Each fuel cell has a number of characteristics, advantages and disadvantages
making them suitable or unsuitable for various applications. The low temper-
ature FC eﬃciencies varies between 40 and 60 %, have quick start up times,
but expensive due to platinum electrolytes and two of three types are very
sensitive towards impurities in the fuel. Due to the low temperatures these
cells are often considered suitable for transportation purposes and other forms
of portable power, as is evident by their utilization in space shuttles[11].
The high temperature FCs are characterised by eﬃciencies upwards of 60 %,
less sensitive to fuel compositions, but suﬀer from sulphur intolerance and have
longer start up times. The MCFC is considered for large scale transportation
such as naval vessels or trains, while SOFCs shows potential as co-generation
for power plants. For more information about fuel cell subtypes, please see the
review of the fuel cell technologies by Kirubakaran et al. from 2009[11] and
Wu et al. from 2006 on CCHP systems with fuel cells among other[29].

20
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND AND TECHNOLOGIES
2.4.3
General working principle
The working principle for the low temperature PEM fuel cell can be seen in ﬁg-
ure 2.9. Hydrogen enters the anode side and decompose into positive charged
hydrogen ions and electrons. The electrolyte membrane allows the hydrogen
ions to travel to the cathode where it reacts with oxygen in the air to create
water. The cathode reactions are exothermic and the dispersed energy leaves
the system as heat. The hydrogen (fuel) oxygen (oxidant) reactions are for-
mulated for the anode, cathode and combined in eq. (2.7), (2.8) and (2.9)
respectively[11].
H2 ⇒2H+ + 2e−
(2.7)
1
2O2 + 2H+ + 2e−⇒H2O
(2.8)
H2 + 1
2O2 ⇒H2O + ∆G
(2.9)
Where ∆G is the change in Gibbs free energy between products and reactants.
The maximum electrical work which can be produced by a fuel cell with no
losses and at constant temperature and pressure are deﬁned as:
Wel = ∆G = −nFE
(2.10)
Where n is number of electrons in the reactions, F is Faraday’s constant and
E16 is the ideal potential of the cell.
The real potential of a fuel cell is decreased by several irreversible losses, as
seen on ﬁgure 2.10.
Figure 2.10: Voltage-current diagram for a fuel cell with activation, ohmic
and concentration losses. Taken from [30].
The activation losses relates to the activation energy of the electrochemical
16Also called ’Electromotive force (EMF) or reversible open circuit voltage.
==PAGE:37==
2.4. FUEL CELLS
21
reactions. These are less prevalent in high temperature fuel cells, such as a
SOFC. The ohmic losses are ionic resistances in the electrolyte and electrodes
(anode and cathode) and are strongly related to the temperature, materials and
geometry of the fuel cell. Concentration losses are mass transport limitations
in the fuel cell and are connected to the utilization factor. The voltage rapidly
decline due to activation losses initially, followed by a more linear decline due
to the resistance losses, as the current is increased. The sharp decline at the
end reﬂects the lower concentration of oxidant and fuel while at high currents.
Combining all the irreversibilities, the operating voltage of the fuel cell is[31]
V = E −∆Vohm −∆Vact −∆Vcon
(2.11)
To avoid the sharp drop in voltage with increased currents, more fuel normally
pass through the fuel cell than can be chemically converted, leading to the fuel
utilization factor[32]:
Uf = Fuel mass reacting in FC
Fuel mass at inlet of FC
(2.12)
For small systems a high fuel utilization factor is normally preferable, as the
fuel cell is normally the only power producing unit in the system. For a larger
system, combined with a gas turbine for example the Uf would be lower to allow
the gas turbine burner temperature to reach an optimal level. Depending on
the fuel cell type and conﬁguration the utilization factor is normally between
0.5 and 0.95 for commercial fuel cells[30].
2.4.4
SOFC characteristics
The SOFC electrolyte is a solid ceramic material which allows oxygen ions to
pass through. Oxygen is reduced to negatively charged ions in the cathode
and pass through the electrolyte to oxidise the fuel in the anode. Figure 2.11
shows the working principle with oxygen ﬂowing through the cathode, oxygen
ions passing through the electrolyte and fuel oxidizing in the cathode[33]. The
surplus free electrons from the oxidation process pass through the load, to the
cathode where they further reduce oxygen to ions. This means that any gas
which can be reduced by oxygen could in theory be utilized as a fuel.
Equation (2.13) shows the reaction in the anode between hydrogen and oxygen
ions.
Eq.
(2.14) and (2.15) shows the reactions for carbon monoxide and
methane respectively. Since the SOFC utilizes a mix of fuels, the combined
reaction become non-trivial and interact, such that carbon monoxide generated
when methane is converted in equation (2.15) could further convert via equation
(2.14). The cathode oxygen reduction is shown in eq. (2.16).
Hydrogen as a fuel, anode reaction:
H2 →H2 + O2−→H2O + 2e−→H2O
(2.13)
Carbon monoxide as fuel, anode reaction:
CO →CO + O2−→CO2 + 2e−→CO2
(2.14)
Methane as fuel, anode reaction:
CH4 →CH4 + 2O2−→H2O + CO + 4e−→CO, H2O
(2.15)
==PAGE:38==
22

Figure 2.11: Working principle of a SOFC.
The oxidation reactions are exothermic in nature, producing a signiﬁcant amount
of energy as heat in the process. This both keeps the fuel cell at the correct
temperature, but also produces a signiﬁcant amount of waste heat leaving the
system with the used fuel (emissions) and the surplus air streams. The cathode
reaction is the same in all cases:
O2 + 4e−→2O2−→O2
(2.16)
The total reaction over the electrolyte only considering hydrogen and carbon
monoxide reactions:
H2 + CO + O2 −→H2O + CO2 + ∆G
(2.17)
2.4.5
Material requirements
The material requirements of the SOFC electrolyte are fairly speciﬁc. It must
be able to conduct oxygen ions while not conducting electrons. Electronic con-
duction in the material has a negative impact on the SOFC eﬃciency, since elec-
trons passing through the electrolyte takes no part in the electrical circuit[33].
Most electrolyte materials increase ion conductivity at increasing temperatures,
but at an even higher temperature become electronic conductive, the interval
determine the electrolyte operation temperature.
The most common mate-
rial YSZ, yttrium stabilized zirconia, conduct oxygen ions between 700 and
1100 ◦C, while becoming electronic conductive at 1500 ◦C [34]. Due to me-
chanical stress in components, it is desirable to operate the SOFC at as low a
temperature as possible.
The relatively high operating temperature of the SOFC is both an advantage
and disadvantage in numerous ways. The high temperature enables internal
reforming in the fuel cell, where CH4 reacts with water to produce carbon
monoxide and hydrogen which occurs simultaneously with eq. (2.15) at a ratio
depending on the temperature and pressure in the anode[35]. Furthermore the
by-product heat produced during reactions is well suited for co-generation with
a bottoming cycle, displaying both both high eﬃciencies and low ghg emissions.
==PAGE:39==
2.5. ABSORPTION COOLING
23
High temperature will on the other hand dictate strict requirements for mate-
rial properties in all components of the fuel cell[30].
Due to the high temperature in the SOFC, the air utilized in the cathode also
serves as a cooling medium to keep the fuel cell at the desired temperature.
The air must however be preheated to avoid large thermal stresses between
cathode, electrolyte and anode. Temperatures around 500 to 700 ◦C is com-
mon and the air is normally preheated via heat exchange with the hot used
oxidant leaving the fuel cell cathode.
2.4.6
Output, cells and stacks
The voltage produced by a single cell in any fuel cell is quite small (between
0.5 and 1.0 V), meaning many cells will have to be set in series, each increasing
the voltage level slightly, to reach a usable voltage level. This series of cells is
called a fuel cell stack. SOFCs are designed in several diﬀerent conﬁgurations,
overarching separated in planar and tubular.
The tubular design is a pipe
shaped cathode with an electrolyte layer and and anode on the outside. The
air/oxidant ﬂows in the pipe in contact with the cathode, while the fuel ﬂows on
the outside in contact with the anode. The design eliminates gas seals between
cells, thus simplifying the fuel cell. The planar are layers stacked on top of each
other with a rectangular shape. The planar design has smaller losses leading
to better performance, but issues with gas seal are signiﬁcant[33]. The planar
conﬁguration is used in this thesis, while tubular is not considered.
The SOFC follows the same theory as the general working principle, but due
to several gas species in the fuel the calculations become fairly complex. For
more information about SOFC reactions and eﬃciency, see Larmine et al.[34].
2.5
Absorption cooling
An absorption cycle utilizes a ’thermal compressor’ instead of mechanical power
to produce cooling or refrigeration, as opposed to a normal vapour compression
cycle which utilizes a mechanically driven compressor. The vapour compres-
sion cycle generally uses a single working media, a refrigerant such as R600a,
ammonia or CO2, while the absorption cycle requires a mixture of water and
a working medium[36]. Compared to the vapour compression cycle, the pump
in the absorption cycle requires signiﬁcantly less mechanical power, but addi-
tionally requires a heat input to drive the compressor.
The terminology often refer to the mixture of refrigerant and absorbent, in
pure, rich or poor, with the concentration ξ. Pure means only refrigerant, the
working medium. Rich solution means a mixture of refrigerant and absorbent
rich on refrigerant, ξr. The poor concentration ξp has a high concentration of
absorbent and low concentration of refrigerant[36].
An absorption cycle main components consists of an absorber, desorber (often
called generator), condenser, evaporator, a pump and two expansion devices.
The cycle can be seen on ﬁgure 2.12 showing how the system can be horizon-
tally divided in a high and low pressure part. The absorber is the low pressure
vessel in which the vapour refrigerant (working medium) and absorbent - com-
ing from the evaporator and desorber - are mixed into a liquid solution. The
==PAGE:40==
24
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND AND TECHNOLOGIES
Cond
Desorb
Evap
Absorb
Heat rejection
Cooling output
Heat input
Heat rejection
Solution valve
Throttle valve
Solution 
pump
Thermal compressor
Figure 2.12: Working principle of an absorption cooling plant.
solution is pumped to the desorber, where the refrigerant is distilled17 by added
heat while the absorbent ﬂows back through an expansion device to the ab-
sorber. The refrigerant rejects heat in a condenser and is brought back to liquid
state. In the following expansion device, the pressure drops back down to the
low pressure level, causing the boiling point of the medium to drop. The low
pressure refrigerant absorbs heat in the evaporator, thus causing the cooling
eﬀect and bringing the refrigerant back to vapour state. From the evaporator
the working medium is dissolved in the absorber, where the process repeats[36].
In order to dissolve the refrigerant into the absorbent, heat must be removed
from the absorber, normally in the form of dry cooling, a cooling tower, sea-,
lake- or groundwater[6].
Two ﬂuid mixtures commonly used are a mixture of ammonia and water (NH3-
H2) or water and lithium bromide (H2O-LiBr), each with well deﬁned proper-
ties.
Ammonia-water is utilized for sub-zero cooling, with ammonia as the refrig-
erant and water as the absorbent. For this mixture a rectiﬁer is required to
remove possible water vapour from the ammonia leaving the desorber. Lithium
bromide-water is used for above-zero cooling and the refrigerant is water, while
the lithium bromide is the absorbent. The Lithium bromide-water is non toxic
as opposed to ammonia and water has a high heat of evaporation, making it
suitable as a refrigerant for the absorption cycle. The pressure in the system
must however be very low to have water boiling at the desired cooling tem-
perature and the lithium-bromide can have issues with crystallization at low
temperature[36]. For comfort cooling, as normally provided by district cooling,
Lithium Bromide is most often used due to the advantages stated above and
the fact that sub-zero cooling is not needed.
The coeﬃcient of performance of an absorption system can be calculated as
the heat removed in the evaporator over the heat supplied in the generator[37].
17By boiling the refrigerant out of the solution.

2.6
District heating and cooling
This project will not go into detail with how the district heating (DH) net-
work is designed and operated, but just in short go over the advantages of
distributing heating and cooling in this manner compared to for example elec-
trical heating. District heating and cooling are often collected under the term
district energy (DE). District energy can be deﬁned as production and distri-
bution of heating and cooling at central or decentralized plants. Today DE is
almost entirely used for comfort heating and cooling of building and hot water
production.
The district heating network is paramount for low energy heating of houses
and the system investigated in this thesis are dependant on the existence of a
local heating distribution network. The ﬂexibility in heat sources and lowering
the fuel consumption in CHP plants is possibly the best argument for utilizing
district heating (and cooling).
The ability to utilize waste heat from other
processes compared to using electricity directly is signiﬁcant in saving primary
fuels and reducing ghg emissions[39].
The current iteration of DH is normally referred to as 3rd generation, while
the upcoming low temperature DH is named 4th generation. 4th generation
==PAGE:42==
26
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND AND TECHNOLOGIES
DH is expected to play a pivotal role in the transition to 100 % sustainable
heating and cooling of buildings in the future, as part of a fully sustainable
energy system. The design process of 4th generation DE changes the focus
from what can be supplied by the producer and focuses instead on the demand
at the consumer. Table 2.2 shows the supply and return temperatures of the
current and future DH system with a ’transition period’ in between. The main
reason for keeping the DH at above 50 ◦C is to avoid legionella, a bacteria
which thrives in hot water at body temperature[40].
Table 2.2: Supply and return temperatures for 3rd and 4th generation, with
a transition temperature interval.
3rd generation
Transition
4th generation
Supply temp. [◦C]
85
70
55
Return temp. [◦C]
50
40
25
One of several further advantages of low temperature district heating (LTDH)
is the reduced heat loss during transmission, a reduction of 75 % compared to
3rd generation DH[41]. Additionally the lower return temperature increases
the eﬃciency of thermal plants and facilitates an even better utilization of low
temperature waste heat from industrial processes, geothermal and solar en-
ergy.
District cooling follows the same principle as district heating, but with one
important distinction - while DH is used for household heating, it is unlikely
that DC will do the same, at least in Scandinavia. Instead district cooling is
normally used by larger building complexes, such as hotels, shopping centres,
oﬃce buildings and other buildings where people, IT equipment or machinery
produces heat. For southern Europe the situation is likely diﬀerent, as these
regions have a signiﬁcant amount of warms days each year.
Instead of cooling water being produced by vapour compression cycles18 locally,
cooling water is delivered by external suppliers. Two temperature sets are often
mentioned, ﬁrst being 4 ◦C supply and 12 ◦C return, second is 6 and 16 ◦C
for supply and return respectively[42]. District cooling is often a combination
of several systems, for example dry cooling (heat exchanger with ambient air),
sea or lake cooling (heat exchanger with cold water available) and vapour com-
pression cycles. The system would utilize the cheapest available cooling when
possible, in most cases the dry cooling. An expedient solution is to produce DC
with surplus heat in an absorption plant, as high cooling demand corresponds
well to low heating demand[6].
The large up front investments of district energy is in general one of the major
issues[39].
18Possibly with the help of dry-cooling.
==PAGE:43==
2.7. SUMMARY
27
2.7
Summary
Interestingly, either of the two reports concerning the future of the Danish en-
ergy system mentions waste incineration in more than passing[14][13]. It would
seem the current waste incineration model is maintained and the focus is in-
stead on recycling and reducing waste. Both methods are decidedly preferable
to the thermal treatment of waste, but a signiﬁcant portion of MSW cannot
be recycled and must be either burned or landﬁlled regardless.
Based on the EU data presented, there is a great potential for waste utilized as
a fuel, particularly in southern and eastern Europe. Landﬁlls are in every way
at poor solution for waste management and uncontrolled landﬁlls contribute
signiﬁcantly to green house gas emissions. One of many obstacles is the lack of
a DH heating transmission grid or low heating demand in warmer climates. For
Denmark speciﬁcally, the current WtE capacity is ageing and leaves decision-
and policy makers in a unique situation when it comes the the future waste
handling system. Furthermore the waste incineration sector is relying on im-
ported waste, a trend which is unlikely to continue.
Gasiﬁcation of MSW is a viable treatment option with several diﬀerent reactor
designs, each with advantages and disadvantages. Some cleaning of the prod-
uct gas is necessary before it can be utilized in the SOFC, but all are possible
with current technology[24].
Solid oxide fuel cells oﬀer high electric eﬃciency and several unique properties
due to the high operating temperature. The ability to reform gas species in-
ternally can signiﬁcantly reduce the need for pre-processing of fuels and thus
simpliﬁes the design. Furthermore the operating temperature makes SOFCs an
excellent candidate for utilizing a bottoming cycle for better energy utilization.
One way to utilize the by-product waste heat of the SOFC is to produce cool-
ing. The cooling can be produced in an absorption cycle when demand for
heating is low, using a thermal compressor instead of mechanical, with the
beneﬁt of very low power consumption. The absorption cycle uses a mixture
of a refrigerant and an absorbent which is distilled in the desorber to produce
a refrigerant vapour.
==PAGE:44==
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 3
MSW as a Fuel
This chapter describes the process of determining physico-chemical composition
and properties of MSW, information needed when modelling MSW as a fuel.
3.1
Fuel composition
Municipal solid waste (MSW) can be seen as a renewable biomass feedstock
utilized for energy production. To determine the potential for use as fuel in
energy systems, the composition of the waste must be evaluated and the heating
value estimated.
Meraz et al. made several observations about MSW as a fuel in 2003[43]. MSW
can be considered as a combination of combustible and incombustible materials.
Food waste, paper, plastics and textiles are organic in nature are the primary
contributors to the combustion. Glass, metal and dirt are in general inorganic
and any combustible material comes from package labels, food scraps and so
forth. The heat released during combustion and moisture content varies greatly
between diﬀerent material fractions, making some materials more suitable for
combustion than others. During combustion most of the inorganic matter ends
up in the ash, resulting in an ash with high metal concentrations.
3.1.1
Methodology
When utilizing solid waste as a fuel in DNA simulations, a speciﬁc input format
is required. The elemental composition based on dry weight basis, moisture
content, speciﬁc heat capacity and heating value. The method for evaluating
the fuel properties is found utilizing the following steps:
• Determine waste fractions for waste type in question, for example house-
hold waste, hotel and restaurant business waste or other. Normally in
percentage or g/kg.
• For each waste fraction, it is necessary to evaluate the chemical element
composition, ash content and moisture content from tabular values.
• Accumulate elemental composition, calculate higher heating value (HHV)
based on correlations for elemental compositions of solid fuels and esti-
mate lower heating value (LHV).
29
==PAGE:46==
30
CHAPTER 3. MSW AS A FUEL
• Calculate speciﬁc heat cp.
• Format for DNA input.

and ﬁgure 3.2 shows the desired DNA format.

In an upcoming article by Götze et al. data from almost a hundred published
articles, reports, books and theses are condensed into an overview of physico-
chemical1 waste characterisation[44]. Some of the major challenges regarding
waste properties are discussed. Much of the analysed literature does not have
waste characterization as the focus and has other research areas concerning
waste as the main topic. Furthermore, the published material often lacks im-
portant information such as whether the data within are on dry or wet basis
or the origin of the waste.
Due to the heterogeneous nature of MSW, the characteristics will vary depend-
ing on numerous factors, such as geographical region, income level, culture and
so forth. The previously mentioned report from the World Bank shows an sig-
niﬁcant diﬀerence in waste fractions depending on income level, as can be seen
in table 3.1[8]. The other category includes rubber, textiles, etc. The major

Determining an average chemical composition and

3.2
Waste fractions
Waste fractions2 or waste classiﬁcation is the practise of dividing the waste into
visibly discernible categories, such as paper, plastics, food waste etc. Table A.2
in appendix A shows various diﬀerent deﬁnitions of waste fractions. As noted
by Götze in [44], no international consensus for waste fractions exists, causing
issues when working with waste fractions from multiple sources. Since no stan-
dard for reporting waste fractions exists, fractions are most often based on the
context of the source material. Statistics Denmark3 has detailed fractions for
all waste produced in Denmark based on various activities, but are unsuitable
for determining elemental composition due to classiﬁcation types[45].
The material fractions used to determine chemical element compositions in
this project was presented in 2015 by Edjabou et al.[46]. The article presents
a detailed study of residual house waste (RHW), the fraction of waste left af-
ter producer sorting of MSW, which is well in line with the applications of
the investigated system. Based on the study a classiﬁcation of waste into 10
separate categories was suggested, each with three levels of detail. The RHW
are separated into food waste, gardening waste, paper, board, plastic, metal,
class, miscellaneous combustibles, inert and special waste.
For the purpose
of determining waste characteristics, paper and board has been merged into
a single category as the fraction is generally listed as such in open literature.
Miscellaneous combustibles include human hygiene waste, textiles, rubber etc.
Inert comprises ashes, gravel, sand and household construction waste. Special
waste is primarily batteries, electronic and electrical equipment and hazardous
waste. The original full table with deﬁnitions can be seen in appendix A table
A.3.
The chemical element composition for each waste fraction is derived from

fraction: Detailed fractions, the components of each waste fraction.
For the food waste
fraction the material fractions are vegetable waste, food scraps. For paper the list of materials
are numerous, for example oﬃce paper, cardboard, kitchen tissues etc.

Moisture and ash content
The moisture content, volatiles and ash of a solid waste fraction sample is
determined by a proximate analysis, in which the sample is ﬁrst heated to
above 105 ◦C, the weight diﬀerence before and after resulting in the moisture
content, see eq.
(3.2).
The remainder of the sample, the total solids (TS)
are then heated to 550 ◦C, where the volatile matter (VM) are combusted.
The remaining part of the sample is the ash. The chemical composition of
the volatiles can be determined using various methods, such as measuring the
amount of CO2 generated during combustion[26].

ferences are signiﬁcant for some fractions, such as paper and board with vary
between 5.5 and 16.2 %. It would seem the simple averages of material waste
fractions overestimate moisture content compared to the typical values, likely
because not all materials are equally present in the waste. For evaluating mois-
ture content, the typical content percentages are used with the assumption that
special waste has zero moisture6.
6The waste fractions of special waste is below 1 % in most cases and the contribution to
the overall moisture content would be insigniﬁcant.

3.4. HEATING VALUE
37
3.4
Heating value
When utilizing MSW as a fuel, the heating values or caloriﬁc values as it is
sometimes called, is a most important thermodynamic parameter. The lower
heating value (LHV) is deﬁned as the amount of heat released for a given
quantity during combustion, bringing the fuel from an initial temperature of
25 ◦C to combustion and the combustion products down to 150 ◦C. Since the
temperature is still above the boiling point of water at atmospheric pressure,
the latent heat of vaporization is not included in the LHV. For the higher
heating value (HHV) the combustion products are brought back to the initial
temperature, meaning the latent heat of vaporization is included. The LHV is
most commonly used when evaluating thermal systems in Europe, while other
countries/regions prefer the HHV.
Table 3.6 shows the LHV for common fuels, biomass materials and MSW. The
table is comprised of multiple diﬀerent sources and thus the deﬁnition of MSW
likely diﬀers, which partly explains the diﬀerences between the heating values.
The MSW samples from the UK is based on an experimental derived analysis
and is calculated from the chemical element composition of the waste, stating
the dry weight heating value[50]. The Danish samples on the other hand are
approximations based on the input and output of large scale Waste to Energy
(WtE) plants and are on a "as received" basis (takes into account moisture
content), resulting in a signiﬁcantly lower heating values compared to the UK
samples. Two types of common plastics are added to the table to illustrate
the LHV of hydrocarbons[51]. In some literature the HHV has a negative sign,
using the convention of work done by the system being negative.
Table 3.6: Lower heating value overview of common fuels and MSW based
on various references.
Fuel

Perhaps the most common correlation for estimating HHV is proposed by Du-
long’s in 1880, derived from coal. Since then a number of corrections to Du-
long’s correlation has been proposed, but the original correlation, see eq. (3.4),
is still utilized today. High oxygen content causes rather large errors in Du-
long’s correlation.

liquid and gaseous fuels on eq. (3.5). The correlation has an absolute error of
1.45 % within the given ranges of elements[54].

Meraz includes moisture content of the fuel in the correlation (wet basis) and
negative sign convention, while eq. (3.4) and (3.5) are on dry basis with pos-
itive sign convention. The moisture content term can simply be omitted for
easy comparison between correlations.
Using the chemical element composition from ﬁgure 3.5 the HHV has been cal-
culated for each correlation (with elements and ash in mass percentages). The
HHV on dry basis for Dulong (ash free), Channiwala and Meraz are 28.1, 22.75
and 23.75 MJ/kg respectively. The major diﬀerences in estimations are the
inclusion of ash content in the Channiwala correlation, but a negligible nitro-
gen coeﬃcient, while the coeﬃcient for oxygen, sulphur and particular nitrogen
are higher for Meraz. Dulong include neither ash nor nitrogen, both present
in signiﬁcant amounts in waste. Based on this, the Channiwala correlation is
utilized to estimate HHV for MSW in this thesis.
The LHV on dry basis can be estimated via eq. (3.7)[55].

Speciﬁc heat capacity
The speciﬁc heat cp is not readily deﬁned for a mixture and is calculated as
a simple weighted average with the speciﬁc heat of each chemical element, eq.
(3.9), based on[55].

3.5
Summary
A method for determining physico-chemical properties of MSW based on waste
fractions has been developed. The method focuses on Danish data for waste
and material fractions, but should apply reasonably well for all countries similar
to Denmark to terms of geography and income level (Northern and Middle
Europe, US, UK etc.).
The method only takes sorting into account in the
waste fractions and only ’common’ waste is considered, such that fractions of
atypical waste from a speciﬁc industry is not supported without some editing.
==PAGE:56==
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 4
System Modelling
The following chapter introduces the modelling of the system. The simulation
tool DNA is brieﬂy described. The modelling of the system, divided in three
sections corresponding to the three main technologies gasiﬁcation, SOFC and
bottoming cycle with absorption plant is presented. The evaluation methods
of the tri-generation plant is discussed and the concept of exergy analysis is
presented.
4.1
Simulation tool
The main simulation program utilized in this thesis is Dynamic Network Anal-
ysis (DNA), a software developed at the Mechanical Department, Technical
University Denmark (DTU) since 1989. The program is written in FORTRAN
and uses an text-based EMACS editor for user input. Through a component
and ﬂuid property library, the user can connect various components to create a
thermodynamic system network. The network is analogue to that of an electri-
cal circuit, connecting components by nodes via branches in between, as seen
on ﬁgure 4.1. Each component is deﬁned via the library with a unique name
and input and output nodes and parameters, governed by a set of energy and
mass balance equations.
Component
1
2
Component
3
4
5
Node
Component
Branch
Figure 4.1: DNA network diagram with 5 nodes and two components. Each
node is deﬁned by a number and each component by a unique name. Here
the ﬁrst component could be a burner and the second a heat exchanger for
example.
41
==PAGE:58==
42
CHAPTER 4. SYSTEM MODELLING
DNA solves steady state systems via an iterative process. Apart from physical
properties being deﬁned as desired (temperatures, pressures, etc.), a number
of reasonable start guesses might be needed for the solver to converge.
A simple heat exchanger has ﬁve nodes which must be connected to a cold
and hot in- and outlet together with a heat loss node which is not connected.
Furthermore two pressure loss parameters has to be deﬁned. For each node a
number of operating conditions must be set, such as pressure, temperature etc.
for DNA to be able to solve the system[57–59].
The thermodynamic system is constructed and simulated in DNA, which also
facilitates parametric variations of all deﬁned inputs and component param-
eters. The commercial software Engineering Equation Solver (EES) is used
as the general calculation program where DNA is not applicable. The pro-
gram is well suited for thermodynamic calculations with an extensive substance
database and iterative equation solver[56]. Microsoft Excel is utilized as stan-
dard data analyser and organizer to process the text-based DNA output ﬁles.
4.2
System modelling
The thermodynamic model for the initial base case plant design can be seen
in ﬁgure 4.2, with the three main sections top cycle gasiﬁcation and SOFC
and the bottoming cycle with absorption plant (APP) and district heating
connection. The absorption plant is here represented by a single block. The
bottoming cycle is a parallel connection between the APP and district heating
production in a simple heat exchanger. The conﬁguration, modelling, inputs
and assumptions of each main section is presented in sections 4.3 to 4.5.
A general assumption for the system is that the production of power, DE and
waste products (ash, sulphur) will be able to be utilized and consumed or re-
moved outside of the system boundary. Furthermore, the transmission and
conversion of power are beyond the system boundaries, likewise the transmis-
sion of DE is not considered. This assumption seems fair as the consumption
is supposed to occur near the system. Regulation and control of the system,
such as part load are not simulated or covered thermodynamically, but makes
for an excellent point of discussion in section 9.5. No components have deﬁned
heat losses, while all components but the absorption cycle has pressure losses.
The system design case is assumed to be operating during the summer, where
the need for district cooling is high and the outside temperature is too high
to adequately be utilized in dry coolers. The outside/ambient temperature is
set to 25 ◦C, a warm day in the Nordic climate. It is assumed that a small
portion of DH is always required to cover immediate hot water consumption
etc. Seasonal variation simulations are only conducted in a limited fashion in
chapter 6, as the bottoming cycle focus is on providing cooling primarily.
The system comprises many diﬀerent components, each with a varying number
of inputs. Advanced components such as the gasiﬁcation reactor and the SOFC
has a considerable amount of inputs, while other more specialized components,
for example the desulphurizer has none. This means that the control of some
components can be very detailed, while others oﬀer no control at all from a
modelling point of view.

CHAPTER 4. SYSTEM MODELLING
4.2.1
Plant size
According to a report from IRENA1 from 2012, integrated biomass gasiﬁcation
- fuel cell plants are still only on the research stage and has several phases of
development remaining before becoming a mature/commercial technology[60].
As a result of this, very little operational experience with MSW gasiﬁcation
integrated with an SOFC is available and many parameters must be based on
assumptions or other theoretically studied systems.
The output of the system must be based on an average waste production at
a given location over a period of time, for example a year.
This can vary
greatly depending on the number of households connected to the system, the
size of the hospital or shopping center. From a thermodynamic point of view
the system has no size limit and could in theory be as large as desirable, but
from a practical engineering point of view both the gasiﬁcation and fuel cell
technology are not ready for large scale production in the immediate future.
Most often gasiﬁcation capacities are reported based on the full system output,
in electricity and district heating, rather than by the input of feedstock and
heating values.
For the small scale plants the gasiﬁcation process is often
combined with a gas engine and a heat recovery system to produce power and
heat and cannot directly be translated to the investigated system[25]. Fixed
bed gasiﬁers seem somewhat limited in scaling opportunities, in particular the
downdraft conﬁguration2. The reactor type can have trouble maintaining a
homogeneous bed for big reactors, further complicated by the heterogeneity of
MSW. The ﬂuidized beds reactors however has a good potential for up-scaling,
the circulating design being the best[10].
While large scale gasiﬁcation systems should be able to replace centralised
WtE plants in the future, the gasiﬁcation technology is still in infancy when it
comes to gasifying waste. Small scale systems seems to be a realistic goal in
the upcoming years, while large scale systems lack operational experience[22].
The system model is designed in such a way that only the MSW mass ﬂow to
the dryer has to be changed for varying the size/scale of the model, all other
mass ﬂows are calculated as necessary.
All components but the SOFC will
adjust as required. The SOFC on the other hand is governed by a number of
stacks and a number of fuel cells in each stack. Typically the number of cells
per stack is constant, but the number of stacks will have to be changed as the
design MSW mass ﬂow changes. For a mass ﬂow of 54 kg/h (0.015 kg/s) the
preliminary model used 150 stacks of 75 cells each. For a larger system, with a
MSW mass ﬂow of 144 kg/h (0.04 kg/s) the system has 350 stacks in the fuel
cell. This is an estimate and subject to further investigation in the chapter 6.
The current capacity possible for the fuel cell technology is around 2 MWel3,
with the SOFC being anywhere from a few kW up to the limit. This is likely
to change with further development of the SOFC in the future[11]. The base
case system is designed with 144 kg/h feed input, around 1100 tons per year
1The International Renewable Energy Agency.
2Some reports suggest 1 MWth input is the limit for a downdraft gasiﬁer. Assuming a
MSW of 10 MJ/kg, that results in a MSW mass ﬂow of 0.1 kg/s.
3The cite article is from 2009 and as such the limit for fuel cell capacity might already
be signiﬁcantly higher than reported here.
==PAGE:61==
4.3. GASIFIER
45
assuming 8000 operational hours/year.
4.3
Gasiﬁer
The gasiﬁcation plant comprises the drying, gasiﬁcation and post-treatment of
the producer gas, along with support utilities such as compressors and heat
exchangers.
Steam

Figure 4.3: Schematic diagram of the gasiﬁcation process. DR = Waste dryer,
GSF = Gasiﬁer, AH = Air heater, SB = Steam blower, SH = Steam heater,
DS = desulphurizer, GB = Gas blower.
The gasiﬁcation plant DNA model can be seen on ﬁgure 4.3. MSW is fed con-
tinuously to a dryer, in which most of the moisture is removed via steam. The
gasiﬁer is fed with the dry MSW and a mixture of hot air and steam, the out-
put is product gas and ashes. The sensible heat in the product gas is used ﬁrst
to preheat air followed by a steam generator, producing steam for the drying
and gasiﬁcation process. The gas is cleaned in a desulphurizer, where H2S is
removed catalytically. The gas-cleaning is not as simple in reality, as noted by
Rokni in [55], stating that the practical aspect of gas-cleaning is more compli-
cated than the thermodynamic representation. Aravind and de Jorg suggests
an extensive gas-cleaning system for biomass gasiﬁcation[24]. Dust ﬁlters are
likely necessary in all cases, but whether all steps of the proposed cleaning is
required for MSW gasiﬁcation must depend on the contaminants at the partic-
ular MSW producer. For purpose of the thermodynamic analysis the thermal
cracking of tars are assumed to happen inside the gasiﬁcation reactor.
The Gasifier component (GSF) is modelled as a simple Gibbs reactor in
DNA. The chemical equilibrium of a reaction is reached once the total free
Gibbs energy is at the minimal value. This principle can be used to evaluate
gas compositions at various pressures and temperatures without considering
chemical reactions. The gasiﬁer model does as such not represent a speciﬁc
reactor type, but rather the general working principle of the gasiﬁcation pro-
cess with partial-oxidation (i.e. not a indirectly heated gasiﬁer). For more
information on the modelling of the gasiﬁer, see [61].
==PAGE:62==
46
CHAPTER 4. SYSTEM MODELLING
The Gasifier has a number of parameters and variables, apart from node con-
nections. The output product gas composition species are deﬁned via integers,
such that of 36 available gasses 8 are present in the producer gas; hydrogen,
nitrogen, carbon monoxide, carbon dioxide, water, hydrogen sulphide, methane
and argon. Furthermore, the pressure, temperature, pressure losses and carbon
conversion factor (see section 2.3) must be deﬁned. Due to an underestimate
of methane content in producer gas, the model is able to by-pass a fraction of
methane such that it does not react during the gasiﬁcation[61][25].
