gas at the outlet of the gasiﬁer reactor is assumed to be the same temperature
as the gasiﬁcation process, although the model allows for the a separate outlet
temperature set point if a speciﬁc gasiﬁer was to be modelled. The carbon con-
version factor is based on [61], which uses the same DNA model of the gasiﬁer.
The desulphurizer is simply assumed to remove all sulphur.
The density of MSW can vary quite signiﬁcantly, thus it is likely that a shred-
der would be needed to process the waste into more manageable sizes before
the drying and gasiﬁcation (this process has no thermodynamic relevance and
is not included).
==PAGE:63==
4.4. SOLID OXIDE FUEL CELL
47
4.4
Solid oxide fuel cell
The SOFC DNA component model is based on planar fuel cells by Topsoe Fuel
Cells A/S and the former Risø National Laboratory and calibrated against ex-
perimental data with good results. From a model point of view, all CO and
CH4 are converted to H2 before the electrochemical reactions, such that only
hydrogen is converted in the anode. The amount of fuel (hydrogen, carbon
monoxide and methane) converted is based on the utilization factor[63].
One of the advantages of integrating the gasiﬁcation process with an SOFC is
the ability to utilize the hot product gas directly. Due to the high operation
temperature of the SOFC the fuel (and air) has to be preheated to avoid prob-
lematic stresses. Since the product gas is already at a fairly high temperature
when leaving the gas cleaning, the amount of preheat required is reduced sig-
niﬁcantly.
The SOFC requires both the inlet of air and fuel to be preheated to avoid
thermal stresses.
The power production of the SOFC is calculated as follows:
PSOF C =ηrevηvUf(LHVH2 ˙ηH2,in
+ LHVCO ˙ηCO,in + LHVCH4 ˙ηCH4,in)
(4.1)
Where Uf is the utilization factor, ηv is the voltage eﬃciency and ηrev is the
reversible eﬃciency deﬁned as change in Gibbs energy over the lower heating
value of the fuel.
ηrev = ∆Gfuel
LHVfuel
(4.2)
For more information about the equations behind the modelling of the SOFC
see [55] and [61]. The thermal eﬃciency can be calculated as the SOFC produc-
tion subtracted the auxiliary power consumption for pumps and compressors
divided by the LHV times the mass ﬂow of the MSW:
ηth = PSOF C −PAux
˙mMSW · LHV
(4.3)
The SOFC and auxiliary equipment setup can be seen in ﬁgure 4.4.
The
product gas coming from the gas cleaning is preheated to 650 ◦C before entering
the SOFC. Air is compressed in an air blower and preheated to 600 ◦C with the
oﬀ-air in the cathode preheater, both inlet temperatures common values when
modelling an SOFC[55][64]. The SOFC operates at slightly above atmospheric
pressure. The gas blower (from the gasiﬁcation section) and air blower have to
overcome component pressure losses in the overall system so that the ﬂue gas
out of the bottoming cycle components are at atmospheric pressure.
The oﬀ-fuel and oﬀ-air is burned in a catalytic burner.
A catalytic burner
oxidise the oﬀ-fuel catalytically in the presence of oxygen (from the oﬀ-air)
and produce heat without conventional combustion taking place. The process
produces a signiﬁcantly cleaner ﬂue gas with far lower content of NOx than
what is otherwise possible[65]. The pressure loss in the burner is assumed to
be zero. The ﬂue gas is utilized in the bottoming cycle to produce DE.

DEratio = ˙mfluegas −˙mfluegas,DH

(4.4)

The district heating in the base case is assumed to be current generation,
with a supply temperature of 90 ◦C and a return temperature of 45 ◦C. Only
the production of DE is considered, the distribution in the area/buildings are
outside the system boundaries. Unless otherwise mentioned, the plant produces
almost entirely cooling, with the DEratio being 0.98. The ﬂue gas pressure
drop in the heat exchanger is 0.05 bars and the ﬂue gas leaves the system at
atmospheric pressure.
4.5.1
Absorption plant modelling
The district energy is provided at 6 ◦C with a return temperature of 16 ◦C,
based on [6]. The distribution of the cold water is not considered.
The absorption plant are in several ways dependent on the manner in which
heat can be removed from the system. The condensing temperature limits the
high pressure level in the system, so that to ensure all the vapour refrigerant
can be condensed. By increasing the pressure in the condenser, heat will have
to be rejected at a lower temperature for the condensation to take place.
Furthermore, the available cooling in the absorber will set the limit for the
concentration of refrigerant (water) which can be absorbed by the absorbant
LiBr - the higher the refrigerant concentration the lower the temperature in the
absorber must be. The lower limit for of refrigerant is deﬁned by crystallization
risk in the high Li-Br concentration connection between desorber and absorber.
The desorber DNA component requires the concentration of Lithium bromide

at the inlet (node 75) and the lithium bromide at the outlet (node 76). The

Figure 4.6 shows the thermodynamic model of the single stage absorption plant
with lithium bromide water. The rich solution (high concentration of refriger-
ant) is pumped to the desorber (node 75) where water (refrigerant) is distilled
via boiling, while the remaining poor liquid solution ﬂows to the absorber (node
76). The vapour (node 70) rejects heat and condensates (node 71) before the
pressure is throttled (node 72). The refrigerant starts to evaporate again by
removing heat from the district cooling chilled water (node 72 and 73). The
vapour refrigerant is condensed and re-absorbed into the poor solution (node
73, 74 and 77).
The heat rejected in the condenser is assumed to be used partly for local hot
water production, but is not hot enough to be useful as 3rd. generation district
heating4. The remaining heat of the refrigerant not used for hot water is con-
densed in the ambient air, e.i. a dry cooler, consequently the heat is assumed
to be removed from the system, but does not represent a valuable output or
input. Either way, the heat rejection in the condenser is rarely considered ei-
ther an input or output when determining the performance of the absorption

4.6
Other components
4.6.1
Heat exchangers
Heat exchangers are modelled as counter ﬂow, either simply with a pressure
drop deﬁned for each side or with one additional parameter, a pinch tempera-
ture. The transferred heat is calculated for either side of the heat exchanger:
˙Q = ˙m1(h1 −h2)
(4.8)
The pinch temperature is found as the minimum of the hot or cold end tem-
perature diﬀerences or at phase change if one occurs. The steam generator in
the gasiﬁcation plant is modelled as a simple heat exchanger as well.
4.6.2
Pumps and compressors
All three fans are modelled as a compressor with a isentropic and mechanical
eﬃciency. The mechanical eﬃciency is 0.98 in all three cases. The isentropic
eﬃciency is calculated from the enthalpy change over the compressor[68]:
ηis = h2s −h1
h2 −h1
(4.9)
The isentropic eﬃciency of the individual compressors and pumps can be seen
in the tables for their respective section. See tables 4.1, 4.2 and 4.3 for the the
gasiﬁcation, SOFC and APP sections.
4.6.3
Support components
Splitters and mixers are modelled as a mass balance over the component with
no pressure losses. For a mixer two inlets and one outlet:
˙m1 · h1 + ˙m2 · h2 = ˙m3 · h3
(4.10)
For the splitter the mass balance is reverse, with two outputs and a single
input. The pressure is the same in all nodes.
4.7
System performance
Each of the main sections have a well deﬁned performance parameter. For the
gasiﬁer, the cold or hot gas eﬃciency is common. For the SOFC the thermal
eﬃciency (also called electrical) is used, while the absorption plant is deﬁned
via a coeﬃcient of performance COP. The cold gas eﬃciency (CGE) is the
relationship between the fuel input and the product gas heating values[22].
CGE = Heating value of cooled product gas
Heating value of fuel
=

LHVfeedstock · ˙mfeedstock
(4.11)
The performance of the SOFC is deﬁned as the thermal eﬃciency, the relation-
ship between power produced and the thermal input.
ηth = Pnet

Where Pnet is the power delivered to the grid by the system when taking
auxiliary systems power consumption into account. The performance of the
absorption plant is presented in section 2.5 eq.

by itself.

ηth =

(4.13)
Where the feedstock in the investigated system is MSW.
When considering the performance of a CHP plant, normally three parameters
are presented. The thermal eﬃciency (same as eq. (4.12)), the overall plant
eﬃciency eq. (4.14) and the alpha value eq. (4.15).
ηCHP = Pnet + Qheating
Qth
(4.14)
α =
Pnet
˙Qheating
(4.15)
The alpha value expresses the ratio between heat and power produced. This is
possibly not viable for a CCHP plant, since heating and cooling (for example
DE) cannot be compared without two additional values (for example α and β),
one to relate power and DE and another to relate DC and DH.
ηCCHP = Pnet + ˙Qheating + ˙Qcooling
˙Qth
(4.16)
A CHP and CCHP eﬃciency is however a somewhat poor performance param-
eter in terms, of optimization, of how well the fuel is utilized. A small decrease
in power production can often increase the heat production greatly. Therefore
as a stand alone performance parameter the CPH eﬃciency would encourage
very high heat production and low power production, which is unlikely to be
a good use of the fuel or economically viable. In essence and from a practical
engineering stand point the best performance parameter would be one of mon-
etary value, considering what the products are worth in economic terms, not
thermodynamically. This is however not a straight forward calculation or well
deﬁned and prices on the power market ﬂuctuate extensively.
A potentially better alternative for optimization is exergy analysis and the
exergetic eﬃciency.
4.7.1
Exergy deﬁnition and analysis
From the ﬁrst law of thermodynamics it is well established that energy cannot
be destroyed or created.
From the second law of thermodynamics however
exergy can be destroyed. An exergy analysis uses the fact that while energy
cannot be destroyed, it can degrade in terms of quality towards the surrounding
==PAGE:70==
54
CHAPTER 4. SYSTEM MODELLING
environment. While energy is a quantitative measure, exergy is a qualitative
measure. Exergy is often deﬁned as maximum possible amount of work which
can be done by a given amount of energy. Exergy, and not energy, is the better
measure for the quality of a certain amount of electricity (1 kJ) compared to
the same amount of heating (1 kJ) in energy terms. As Bejan et al. puts
it: ”Electricity clearly has the greater quality, and not incidentally, the greater
economic value.”[69]. The concept of exergy can be useful for determining losses
in a system and individual components and for evaluating the performance of
an integrated system with several outputs/products[69][70].
Exergy is calculated against a ’dead state’ or ’reference state’, where the system
is in equilibrium with the surrounding environment in terms of temperature T0,
pressure P0 and chemical composition µ0. The chemical composition is a set
of reference substances at a reference concentration. For gasses, the reference
substance is often air with a concentration of the common components found
in air. A system in total equilibrium with the surrounding environment has no
exergy, as no driving forces are present to drive processes[70].
The nomenclature for exergy and exergetic analysis is not standardized and can
be very diﬀerent depending on author. The nomenclature used in this thesis is
presented in [71]. Where exergy is denoted E in kJ/s, speciﬁc exergy denoted
e in kJ/kg and exergetic eﬃciency uses ϵ (Greek lower case Epsilon).
Exergy
Physical
Chemical
Potential
Kinetic
Reactions
Temperature
Mechanical
Mix and 
separation
Thermo-mechanical
Pressure
Figure 4.7: The exergy deﬁnition separated into major categories. Figure
inspired by [72].
Figure 4.7 visualizes the deﬁnition of exergy. On a general level exergy can be
split into a physical and chemical component.
E = EP H + ECH
(4.17)
The physical is subdivided into mechanical and thermo-mechanical, each repre-
senting diﬀerent aspects. For a system with no elevation and in rest compared
to the environment6, the kinetic and potential terms of the physical exergy can
be neglected.
EP H = ET M + 
:0
EP T I
+ 
:0
EKNI
(4.18)
The remaining physical exergy ’thermo-mechanical’ is deﬁned as the enthalpy
diﬀerence compared to the dead-state subtracted the dead-state temperature
6As is the case in this thesis.
==PAGE:71==
4.7. SYSTEM PERFORMANCE
55
times the entropy diﬀerence.
EP H = (H −H0) −T0(S −S0)
(4.19)
Entropy is an expression of ’disorder’ in a thermodynamic system and a mea-
sure of irreversibility occurring in real processes.
The entropy of a system
increases due to irreversible processes, such that at thermodynamic equilib-
rium the entropy of a system is maximized.
In some cases the physical exergy is estimated as the energetic content times
the Carnot factor. This value only takes the temperature term of the thermo-
mechanical exergy into account, thus if the pressure levels are not at the refer-
ence pressure P0 this estimate results in faulty values.
The chemical energy is deﬁned as reaction exergy and none-reaction exergy
(mixing and separation). The chemical exergy is the exergy a substance can
perform when not at the reference state.
ECH = ER + EN
(4.20)
Without diving too deep into chemistry, the chemical exergy is conveniently
equal to the higher heating value (as long as the pressure and temperature are
at the reference state)[69][70].
DNA is able to calculate the physical and chemical exergy and speciﬁc exergy
in and out of all components based on a deﬁnable reference state using eq.
(4.19) and (4.20).
4.7.2
Exergetic eﬃciency
The exergetic eﬃciency of a process is deﬁned via the exergy balance. The
exergy of the fuel EF used to generate a product EP is equal to the sum of the
product, the exergy destruction ED and the exergy lost EL.
EF = EP + ED + EL
(4.21)
Exergy losses are exergy lost with material streams leaving the system or leak-
ages of heat. The exergy destruction is related to the entropy generation:
ED = T0 · Sgen
(4.22)
1
2
3
Figure 4.8: Compressor or pump process with a pressure lift of a ﬂuid and a
work input.
For a component the exergetic eﬃciency can be deﬁned as the product EP over
the fuel input EF :
ϵ = EP
EF
(4.23)
==PAGE:72==
56
CHAPTER 4. SYSTEM MODELLING
For the compressor (or pump) on ﬁgure 4.8 the exergetic eﬃciency is:
ϵ = E2 −E1
E3
= m1(e2 −e1)
W
(4.24)
Here the work/electricity supplied to run the compressor is the fuel and the
exergy increase between before and after is the exergy product. The products
and fuels must be identiﬁed individually for all relevant component types in
the system. For a heat exchanger the product would be the exergy diﬀerence
on the cold side and the fuel would be the exergy diﬀerence on the hot side or
vice versa.
Two additional ratios concerning exergy can be very helpful when evaluating
a system. The exergy destruction ratios yD and y∗D:
YD =
ED
EF,tot
(4.25)
The exergy destruction ratio yD expresses how much of the exergy destruction
occurring in a given component compared to the total system fuel input. The
other ratio, y∗

destroyed in the system[69].
Y ∗

ED,tot
(4.26)
4.7.3
Total exergy eﬃciency
The system exergy eﬃciency is very useful when determining the system per-
formance. Exergy is a homogeneous quantity, while energy is not, meaning
that the exergetic value of heating, cooling, power and so forth can freely be
added up and compared. The system exergetic eﬃciency is not always well
deﬁned and varies somewhat depending on literature. Thus when comparing
eﬃciencies, the reference point and the overall deﬁnition is of the utmost im-
portance.
Bejan et al. deﬁnes the exergetic eﬃciency of a system as the sum ’useful’
outputs or products over the sum of inputs.
ϵsys =
P Exergy of useful products
P Exergy of inputs
(4.27)
For a CHP consisting of a gas turbine and steam cycle producing power and
heat, the useful products would be the power outputs from the gas and steam
turbine and the exergetic increase of the heating medium. The input would
be the fuel and possibly the air, depending on the reference state and air inlet
temperature[69].
The values of heating and cooling can be quite low in terms of exergy. This is
due to the small temperature diﬀerence between the product and the reference
state.
==PAGE:73==
4.8. SUMMARY
57
4.7.4
Exergy eﬃciency calculator
A simple Excel spreadsheet was developed to quickly and with very few user
input evaluate the exegetic eﬃciency of a DNA simulated system. The spread-
sheet prompts the user for the DNA simulation output ﬁle (with extension .sta)
and the necessary information is extracted from the raw data automatically.
The user will only have to type in the DNA node numbers for in- and out-put
of the heating or cooling they wish to include in the system exegetic eﬃciency.
Several exergetic streams can be included, such that a system with both heat-
ing and cooling or multiple heating instances can be evaluated.
Excel was decided upon as it is the most widespread calculation tool and fairly
common for quick data analysis tasks. Matlab was another viable option, but
harder to access outside of academia and more complex, which makes expand-
ing the spreadsheet troublesome without extensive coding experience.
See the developed spreadsheet in appendix F, ﬁgure F.1.
4.8
Summary
The initial system design has been presented with the three main sections,
top cycle gasiﬁcation and SOFC with bottoming cycle absorption pant. The
simulation tools utilized in the thesis is brieﬂy introduced, with DNA being
the main simulation program in which the thermodynamic model is build. The
modelling of each section is described together with assumptions and input
parameters.
Methods for evaluating system performance has been shown and discussed.
The exergy eﬃciency could be a better candidate compared to combined cycle
eﬃciencies, with regards to energy optimization.

As mentioned in the secion 4.7 comparing power with heating and cooling are
by some considered a somewhat poor practise. While power is a homogeneous
product, heat and cooling is not. The temperature level at which the DE is
produced has a signiﬁcant impact on how well the available energy in the fuel
is utilized. The better way of combining various outputs is to utilize the exergy
values. The exergetic eﬃciency describes how much of the available energy in
the fuel is utilized in the system and takes temperatures of DE into account.
The exergy ﬂows are evaluated with a reference temperature and pressure of 25
◦C and 1.013 bar respectively. Grosso et al. did an analysis of energy recovery
from waste incineration in Europe in 2010, calculating exergetic eﬃciencies
for the various countries under assumptions of the temperature of the DH
produced. While the method is not exactly the same as the method in this
thesis, some comparisons can be made. The average exergy eﬃciency for a

31.7 % found for the investigated system[73].

5.2
Improvement strategy
As the system is integrated, it is not possible to look at each section separately.
A change in the gasiﬁer will aﬀect the SOFC operation which could in turn
impact the APP cooling production. In light of this, a true optimization of
operational parameters is not possible. Instead parameters must be studied
separately and a combination of individually optimal parameters will form a
pseudo-optimal design. The study will be evaluated based on production of
power and DE, thermal and exergetic eﬃciencies. A parametric study of key
operational parameters and assumptions are conducted in chapter 6, with a
’optimized’ system as a result. Chapter 8 investigates the component exergy
eﬃciencies of the optimized system, in order to determine problematic compo-
nents and possibly inexpedient design choices.
Before considering individual parameters the overall system design should be
evaluated and if necessary redesigned. Two areas of interest in particular are
the anode preheating with or without methanation, and the bottoming cycle
conﬁguration.
5.3
Anode conﬁguration
A methanator is a ﬁxed or ﬂuidized bed reactor with a catalytic bed material,
which at high temperatures encourage the hydrogenation of carbon oxides to
methane in two reactions:
2H2 + 2CO →CH4 + CO2
∆HR = −247
kJ/kmol
(5.3)
4H2 + CO2 →CH4 + 2H2O
∆HR = −165
kJ/kmol
(5.4)
Both reaction (5.3) and (5.4) have negative reaction enthalpy, thus being
exothermic and will increase the temperature of the product gas. The amount
of methane produced and temperature increase depends on both temperature
and pressure in the reactor[74].
The higher amount of methane1 will increase the internal reforming in the fuel
cell, an endothermic reaction consuming heat, lowering the requirement for
cooling air in the SOFC and as an extension lowering the compressor work.
Due to the exothermic reactions in the methanator the preheating of the pro-
ducer gas before entering the SOFC anode might not be required before reach-
ing acceptable temperature levels. Figure 5.1 shows three possible anode pre-
heat conﬁgurations with oﬀ-fuel heat exchanger from the base system (a), oﬀ-
fuel heat exchanger and methanator (b) and methanator (c).
1From around 0.01 to 0.05 mole methane for DK-waste01, see ﬁgure 5.3.

The power production by the SOFC is not changed in any meaningful way
by integrating the methanator, but as expected the endothermic internal re-
forming reduces the auxiliary power consumption for the conﬁgurations with
a mathenator (conﬁg.
(b) and (c)).
The result is a net power production,
thermal and exergetic eﬃciency increase.
The auxiliary power consumption for the three conﬁgurations can be seen on
ﬁgure 5.2, showing the distribution of power consumption in the system. The
auxiliary power consumption of producer gas blower are lower in conﬁg. (b)
as no heat exchange with the oﬀ-fuel occurs (and thus no pressure loss in that
component). The cathode air blower is the prime power consumer for each
conﬁguration. The diﬀerence in conﬁg. (b) and (c) are due to the lower fuel
temperature at the anode inlet, which is aiding with the cooling of the SOFC,
lowering the need for cooling air. This could indicate that the SOFC is oper-
ating at a higher temperature than what is optimal with the current fuel and
should be investigated further in chapter 6.
As the burner outlet temperature increases, so will the potential in the bottom-
ing cycle, which can be seen by the higher DC production. Thermodynamically
the diﬀerence between conﬁguration (b) and (c) is minute, but conﬁg. (c) will
require another component in the heat exchanger, thus complicating the sys-

From the above analysis conﬁguration (b) would seem to be the most ther-

mented in the following considerations. The low anode fuel inlet temperature
is a concern, as the temperature diﬀerence here is above 200 between inlet
and operation temperature, which could cause thermal stresses decreasing the
component lifetime2. This concern should however be at least partly mitigated
by the theory that the SOFC operating temperatures should be reduced in the
base case.
5.4
Product gas composition
The molar gas composition of DK-waste01 from the method section can be
seen in ﬁgure 5.3, visualizing the decrease in hydrogen and carbon monoxide
to produce methane in the methanator. Methane increases from around 0.01
molar fraction to 0.05. The ﬁgure additionally shows the extent of nitrogen
dilution of the product gas.
Figure 5.4 shows the corresponding change in heating values through the pro-
cess steps. The desulphurizer has no noticeable impact on the lower heating
value, but the methanator will trade oﬀheating value potential for a temper-
ature increase and methane production. The oﬀ-fuel potential can be seen,
which strongly depends on the fuel utilization factor, here at 70 %. The heat-
ing value of the ﬂue gas is zero after the catalytic burner.

absorption plant cannot operate and would have to be bypassed entirely, thus
only producing DH. When only producing DC or DH the production is 321.45

The waste fractions of the Danish waste types are seen in table 5.5 and the
moisture content, ash and chemical elements of the waste fuels can be seen on
table 5.6. As expected, the two household wastes are very similar in fractions
and element composition, despite the years between analysis. The health ser-
vices waste contains a huge amount of paper and board, lowering the moisture
signiﬁcant. This is most likely due to the massive amount of sterilized equip-
ment in packaging and other disposable material such as paper towels etc. The
’shopping’ waste follows a similar trend. As a consequence of high amounts of
paper and board and low food waste fraction, the moisture contents are almost
half compared to households. The hotel and restaurant waste are fairly similar
to the household waste, perhaps not unexpected considering both are living
accommodations in some form.

Zhuo et al.[49] and the RDF3 pellets utilized for simulations by Rokni in [55].
These illustrate the extremes of solid waste as a fuel with very high and low
moisture content.

resulting diﬀerence between fuel lower heating values is over 30 % excluding the
MSW from China. From eq. (3.5) in section 3.4 it is clear that hydrogen and
carbon contribute the most to the overall heating value. The combination of a
high carbon and hydrogen content together with low ash and moisture yields
the best heating values. The RDF pellets and the health care services both
have low moisture content making them signiﬁcantly better as fuels compared
to the household wastes and in particular the average waste from China. The
two largest contributors to Chinese waste are food residue and non-combustible
material, making the untreated waste poorly suitable as a fuel.
The method of calculating waste characteristics appear to be working as ex-
pected, such that MSW with a large fraction of plastics and paper and board
result in signiﬁcantly higher heating values. MSW with a large fraction of food
waste on the other hand prove to be less viable fuel, mostly related to the high
amount of moisture.
5.6.1
Simulation results
Each of the fuels have been fed to the revised base plant (ﬁgure 5.7) and the
results are shown in table 5.8. The ﬁrst thing to notice is the lack of a sim-
ulation for the Chinese MSW - the waste was simply too poor a fuel for the
model to converge4, both due to a massive amount of moisture and ash in the
fuel - only around 25 % of the wet weight is useful matter in terms of energy
production. Combined with a somewhat poor heating value, the consequence
is that a signiﬁcantly modiﬁed plant would be required to utilize MSW from
China. For China a plant for thermal treatment of MSW would likely be more
focused on disposal of MSW to reduce landﬁll issues (space used and environ-
mental) compared to energy recovery in other regions.
Each simulation is run for the same mass ﬂow of waste (0.04 kg/s), thus the
thermal input varies correspondingly. The diﬀerence in net power production
is signiﬁcant, the base case fuel DK-01 being somewhere in the middle, thus
a well representing fuel. Depending on the location and scale of the plant, it
is obviously possible that a combination of several sources might be producing
for the same plant, thus the fuel can easily be a mix of several or all of the
activities presented investigated in this thesis.

5.7. SUMMARY
71
The thermal eﬃciency shows a surprising result. The eﬃciency is highest for
what was to be expected as the least suitable fuel - the waste from the hotel
and restaurant business. This fuel has the highest moisture content (apart from
the Chinese) and as a result the lowest lower heating values. This is another
indication that the fuel cell number of stacks is currently too low and that the
plant is unable to utilize the energy available in the fuel for the other cases
with higher LHV.
The DE produced5 is connected to the low thermal eﬃciency, such that the fuel
not adequately utilized in the SOFC will be converted to heat in the catalytic
burner. Therefore, the low thermal eﬃciency results in a temperature increase
in the burner, which in turn directly translates into a higher DE production.
This correlates well with the thermal eﬃciency for DK-HotelRes which pro-
duces the least DE by a fair margin. The COP of the absorption plant is very
similar in all cases, as the parameter is strongly related to the temperatures
which do not change signiﬁcantly when alternating between the waste fuels.
The exergetic eﬃciency does not yield any conclusive results. A high thermal
eﬃciency does not necessarily equal a high exergetic eﬃciency. The number of
parameters changed when varying fuels are fairly extensive, both the chemical
composition of the product gas, the heating values and the moisture and ash
content are diﬀerent and thus the eﬀect is not obvious.
Table 5.8 shows that waste from various activities has a profound eﬀect on the
system. This only reinforces the fact that a good estimate of the waste char-
acteristics are key in the design process of any system operating with any fuel
derived from MSW. This sensitivity towards waste properties and composition
could prove a potential problem for the system, as MSW are expected to vary
signiﬁcantly
5.7
Summary
The initial results for the system has been presented, showing a thermal eﬃ-
ciency of 34.0 %, a signiﬁcant improvement over the conventional WtE plants
operating in Denmark. The APP displays a COP of 0.71, correlating well with
the theory on single stage absorption plants. The exergetic eﬃciency is a big
improvement compared to the average in Europe for conventional WtE. Over-
all the system produces a fair margin more electricity than a WtE plant, but
less DE.
The anode preheating conﬁguration shows that the methanator can improve
the system by replacing the anode preheater heat exchanger entirely, though
without considering the economic aspects. The initial design was revised to
include the methanator.
Due to the temperature in the desorber and DH heat exchanger being very
similar, the bottoming cycle connection has a quite small impact on produc-
tion and eﬃciencies.
5The reason for the almost identical DH production is likely due to a rounding error for
the mass ﬂows. When the plant produces almost only DC the mass ﬂow of DH becomes very
small and thus the error is introduced.
==PAGE:88==
72
CHAPTER 5. DESIGN RESULTS
The conﬁguration changes conducted in this chapter will be revisited and ver-
iﬁed for the optimal system in the ﬁnal section of chapter 6.
The method for evaluating diﬀerent MSW types has been applied to ﬁve fuel
types, showing good correlations with expected behaviour. The fuel consid-
erations clearly shows that waste produced during various activities will have
a signiﬁcant impact on the system production and performance. In light of
this, a good estimate of waste fractions is paramount in the feasibility phase
of designing a plant utilizing waste as a fuel. The results further conﬁrm that
the system SOFC is too small to properly utilize the fuel potentials of the bet-
ter fuels for power production fully. The exergetic eﬃciency does not for the
diﬀerent fuels show any discernible trend.
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 6
Parametric Study
Here follows the parametric study of operational parameters expected to be
important for the performance of the system. This is primarily assumed values
of temperatures, eﬃciencies and so forth.
Unless otherwise stated all simulation parameter studies uses the standard sys-
tem with methanator, Waste-DK01 and parallel bottoming cycle conﬁguration
with the DEratio being 0.98. This conﬁguration is simply called ’base case’ in
the remainder of this chapter.
The objective is to maximize the eﬃciency of the system, getting the most
performance out of the waste fuel.
Due to the deﬁnition of exergetic eﬃciency it will in most cases follow the ther-
mal eﬃciency quite closely, in such a way that an improvement to the SOFC
operation and production is also an improvement to the exergetic system eﬃ-
ciency. There are obviously exceptions to this reasoning, such as improvements
to the bottoming cycle, which will not impact the SOFC operation.
The performance parameters (ηth,ϵsys and COP) are all at design load under
steady state conditions.
6.1
SOFC stack and temperature
The base case stack number is 350 and the default SOFC operation temper-
ature is 800 ◦C. As considered in section 5.3 and 5.6 the temperature of the
SOFC is likely too high, while the stack number might be too low.
Varying the number of stacks in the SOFC (still 75 cells in each stack), the
thermal eﬃciency and exergetic eﬃciency can be seen on ﬁgure 6.1, while ﬁgure
6.2 shows the corresponding power and DE production. The thermal eﬃciency
increases more than the exergetic eﬃciency with an increase in stacks, this is
caused by electricity having a far higher exergetic value than district energy.
This agrees with the situation on ﬁgure 6.2, where the power and district en-
ergy gradients are quite similar.
As can be seen, the stack number alone does not yield any conclusive data
about an optimum, but rather the relationship between Power and DE pro-
duced. The number of stacks must be increased greatly before a substantial

decline in thermal eﬃciency gradient can be observed, as shown in appendix C
ﬁgure C.2.
Figure 6.3 shows the thermal eﬃciency as a function of SOFC operating tem-
perature, using four diﬀerent stack counts. The ﬁgure shows a clear optimum
in the temperature range for each SOFC stack count. Furthermore, conﬁrming
that an operation temperature of 800 ◦C was indeed too high. By lowering
the temperature of the SOFC the demand for cooling also decreases, thus in-
creasing the net power production. The thermal eﬃciency will decline if the
temperature drops too low as the SOFC power production starts to decline,
while the air blower power consumption increases. This is likely due to the
endothermic internal reforming reactions in the SOFC starting to decline from
the lack of available heat. These reactions requires a high temperature to occur
and thus lowering the temperature will decrease the amount of reactions taking
place.
The results from ﬁgure 6.3 are combined on ﬁgure 6.4 to show the trend of
optimum for the four stack counts. The trend indicate that for a decreasing
stack count, the best SOFC operating temperature increases slightly. By in-
creasing the number of stacks, more carbon monoxide in the product gas will
be reformed internally, thus lowering the temperature by consuming heat and

CHAPTER 6. PARAMETRIC STUDY
decreasing the requirement of air cooling and as an extension the air blower
compressor work. An added beneﬁt of the lowering the SOFC temperature is
to minimize the thermal stress in the fuel cell experiences due to the tempera-
ture diﬀerences between inlets and outlets.
The optimal SOFC operating temperature can improve the base case almost 1
percent point for the 350 stacks at ≈730 ◦C, and over 1.5 percent points if the
stacks is increased to 500 at ≈725 ◦C. Whether the increase can compensate
for the increased costs of the SOFC is unlikely, especially considering that the
lost work potential is partly recovered in the bottoming cycle. The lower op-
erating temperature of the SOFC should diminish the concern about thermal
stresses due to the temperature diﬀerence between anode fuel inlet and SOFC
temperature.

The utilization factor Uf was initially assumed be to 0.7. This parameter is
expected to have signiﬁcant impact on the power and DE production relation-
ships.

6.2. SOFC UTILIZATION FACTOR
77
Figure 6.5 shows the eﬃciencies as a function of the fuel utilization factor, with
ﬁgure 6.6 showing the corresponding energy production. The thermal and ex-
ergetic eﬃciency behave quite similarly and the optimal Uf is around 0.715. At
low utilization factors an increase has a positive eﬀect on the power production
and thus the opposite on the bottoming cycle. Further more, for low Uf fewer
reactions will take place in the SOFC, reducing the amount of produced heat
inside the fuel cell and the need for SOFC air cooling. As the utilization factor
increases more reactions take place and the SOFC will heat up further, requir-
ing more air ﬂow to cool it, but the net power production and eﬃciencies are
still increasing. At a certain point however the SOFC experience a sharp drop
in both eﬃciency and production. The cause of this sharp drop is visualized
on ﬁgure 6.7.
0.5
0.6
0.7
0.8
500
600
700
800
900
SOFC fuel utilization factor [-]
Current density [mA/cm2]
500
600
700
800
900
0.5
0.6
0.7
0.8
Current density [mA/cm2]
Cell voltage [V ]
Figure 6.7: Left: Current density of the SOFC as a function of fuel utiliza-
tion factor. Right: Current density impact on cell voltage (voltage/current
characteristics).
The left part of ﬁgure 6.7 shows the current increase in the SOFC as the Uf
increases, while the right shows the voltage/current characteristics. As the Uf
increases, so does the current density of the SOFC, resulting in the voltage
experiencing a sharp drop due to primarily concentration losses and to some
extend ohmic losses. The concentration losses increases as the SOFC has lim-
ited mass transport capabilities. This behaviour agrees well with the theory
presented in 2.4 ﬁgure 2.10.
The fuel utilization factor is discussed by [30] in relation to fuel cell systems.
Depending on the focus of the plant, diﬀerent strategies can be applied. For a
fuel cell only producing power (normally very small scale), the optimal opera-
tion point would behave as presented on ﬁgure 6.5 - a balance between SOFC
production and cooling requirement - but if the plant uses a bottoming cycle it
might be more advantageous to operate at a lower fuel utilization factor, both
lowering the air cooling requirement of the SOFC and increasing the potential
of the bottoming cycle. For a plant with a fuel cell and a gas turbine bottoming
cycle it is likely that a much lower Uf would prove optimal.
==PAGE:94==
78
CHAPTER 6. PARAMETRIC STUDY
For the system investigated in the thesis, the focus is the highest energy (and
thus exergy) eﬃciency, in which case the utilization factor should be selected
in such a manner which most beneﬁt the electricity production.
6.3
Gasiﬁcation temperature
The temperature for gasiﬁcation (GSF) of MSW is normally between 800 and
900 [62], Arena et el. uses temperatures in the 800 to 900 range for their bed
material experiments as well[27]. In the base case the temperature is set to
800. Here follows investigations of the gasiﬁcation temperature impact on the
base case plant operation. The assumption about the product gas outlet tem-
perature being equal to the gasiﬁcation temperature is still valid.

ture would likely have caused a larger degree of tar cracking thus making more
of the feedstock available in the heating value. The increase in temperature
would however also have consumed more of the light gasses, thus a balance
between the two would have been expected.

Figure 6.10: Left: Molar product gas composition out of the gasiﬁer. The
change in Argon and Sulphide concentration is negligible, the molar fraction
being ≈3.5 · 10−3 and ≈4 · 10−4 respectively. Right: Lower heating value of
the product gas as a function of gasiﬁcation temperature.
At higher temperature the reactor requires more air, which causes a further
dilution of the product gas with nitrogen and argon. The higher mass ﬂow
of product gas will increase the need for SOFC cooling and as an extension
increase the compressor work of the air blower. At the same time, the district
energy production will increase slightly due to the higher mass ﬂow passing
through the SOFC and catalytic burner.
To summarize the increased gasiﬁcation temperature will result in a lower prod-
uct gas heating value. Together with the increased product gas temperature
and additional cooling demand, the eﬃciencies of the system will decline with
increasing GSF temperature. The trend for lowering gasiﬁcation temperature
is similar, with a decrease in GSF temperature the thermal eﬃciency will in-
crease, this is however beyond the gasiﬁcation temperature interval presented
and as such not a viable change.

6.4
Carbon conversion factor
The carbon conversion factor (CCF) is in the base case set to 100 %.
In
reality the gasiﬁer is unlikely to reach perfect carbon conversion. For a partial-
oxidation reactor, the CCF can however reach very high values, between 95
and 99 %[21].

The CCF expressed how much of the carbon in the feedstock material is con-
verted into gas products. The remaining carbon leaves the gasiﬁer with the
ash. The eﬀect on the thermal and exergetic eﬃciency is limited in the given
range of CCF, as can be seen on ﬁgure 6.11, both decreasing around 1 per-
cent point for a 5 % drop in CCF. The thermal and exergetic eﬃciency follows
the same declining gradient as both power and DE are equally aﬀected by the
change.
6.5
Turbo machinery eﬃciencies
The isentropic eﬃciencies of turbo machinery is assumed to values between
0.7 and 0.8 for compressors (the air blowers) and 0.9 and 0.95 for the pumps
respectively.
Figure 6.12 shows that the compressor eﬃciencies does not have a signiﬁcant
impact on the system performance. In particular the steam and gas blowers
variation of isentropic eﬃciency has very little eﬀect. Decreasing the isentropic
eﬃciency by 20 % will at the most decrease the thermal eﬃciency by 0.50 %
relatively to the base case (from 0.3506 to 0.3491). The air blower contribute
with the highest power consumption by a fair margin, so the eﬀect of changing
the isentropic eﬃciency is higher. For a 20 % increase in eﬃciency, the thermal
eﬃciency would increase 1 % relative to the base case (from 0.3506 to 0.3542).
The solution pump in the absorption plant has a very low power consumption
due to the very low pressures and therefore the change in isentropic eﬃciency
is negligible, as can be seen on ﬁgure 6.13. The pump supplying the absorber
with cooling water has some eﬀect on the thermal eﬃciency only if the pump
has a extremely poor isentropic eﬃciency.

6.6

Considering only the air and MSW inlet temperatures, the system experiences
very little change to the thermal eﬃciency, as shown on ﬁgure 6.14. Both the
gasiﬁcation and SOFC operate at temperatures far above the ambient temper-
ature and as such, any realistic ambient temperature will have only minute
eﬀect on the thermal eﬃciency.

With varying climate also comes a changed demand in district energy. While

interconnected with the overall district energy system is however a diﬀerent
kind of analysis and beyond the scope of this thesis.
6.7
Other considerations
Here follows an investigation of component pressure drops, inlet temperatures
and waste properties and the eﬀect on the thermal eﬃciency.
Table 6.1 shows the impact on extreme pressure drops in components and vari-
ation of temperatures on the thermal eﬃciency. The cathode pressure drop in
the SOFC has a profound eﬀect on the performance, compared to the pres-
sure drop over the anode. This is due to the large diﬀerence in mass ﬂows on
the two sides of the SOFC. The cathode preheater (CP) pressure drops are
also connected to the relatively large mass ﬂow of air. The mass ﬂow diﬀer-
ence between cooling air entering and leaving the SOFC explains the diﬀerence

in impact on ηth between hot and cold side pressure drop (oxygen ions pass
through the electrolyte to the anode side, see ﬁgure 2.11).
The catalytic burner pressure ratio between inlet and outlet increases the power
consumption of both the air blower and the gas blower. With declining pres-
sure ratio, more and more power has to be supplied to keep the ﬂue gas at
atmospheric pressure at the outlets in the bottoming cycle. The desulphurizer
pressure drop is not as detrimental as the cathode pressure drop, simply be-
cause the mass ﬂow of product gas from the gasiﬁcation plant is signiﬁcantly
lower than the mass ﬂow of air.
The drying temperature has no real impact in the interval. At higher temper-
atures, the feedstock could start pyrolysis which is not desirable. The steam
temperature for the drying and gasiﬁcation does not inﬂuence the SOFC opera-
tion to a large extend either. The cathode inlet temperature on the other hand
should not be increased too much, as this will increase the mass ﬂow greatly
to provide the same amount of cooling for the fuel cell. Thermodynamically
there is no issue with decreasing the air temperature, thus minimizing the air
ﬂow through the cathode and air blower power consumption, but the tempera-
ture diﬀerence between SOFC operation temperature and air inlet should not
exceed a reasonable level to avoid thermal stresses in the fuel cell.

6.8
Absorption plant
The absorption plant has several parameters which should be investigated. The
changes done to the APP parameters will in most cases not inﬂuence the SOFC
and gasiﬁer operation. An exception would be a change in pump eﬃciency or
component pressure drops on the ﬂue gas side. The pump eﬃciencies have
already been investigated in section 6.5.
If DC has to be provided at a lower temperature base case, the low pressure
level of the APP must be adjusted as per ﬁgure 2.13 in section 2.5. The supply
and return temperature of 6 and 16 ◦C is based on common values for comfort
cooling.
6.8.1
Condenser temperature
If the low temperature available heat in the APP condenser is cooled in the
ambient temperature and not used for hot water production, it can be a po-
tential improvement of the system. The means of which the heat is removed
is not implemented into the model however, but simply simulated by lowering
the temperature in the condenser. By having the ambient temperature avail-
able in the condenser, the condensation pressure can be decreased accordingly,
meaning more refrigerant can be able to be condensed.

As previously mentioned the exergetic eﬃciency change with an increase in DC
production is very small. An increase in DC production of around 12.5 kW by
lowering the ambient temperature only increases the exergetic eﬃciency from
0.3281 to 0.3292. The COP better shows the eﬀect of lowering the ambient tem-
perature, increasing from about 0.71 to 0.75 when as the temperature drops.
The solution pump power consumption decreases as well, but the change is
insigniﬁcant.
6.8.2
Refrigerant concentration and absorber temperature
As mentioned earlier, the refrigerant concentration is related to the available
temperature in the absorber, which limits to what extend the lithium bromide
can absorb the water refrigerant. As the absorber temperature is lower, more
refrigerant can be absorbed which in turn will increase the DC production. In
the base case the cooling water inlet in the absorber is 20 ◦C sea or lake water
pumped to the system. With a 45 % concentration of refrigeration in the rich
solution (ξr = 0.45) in the base case, the temperature required to absorb the
refrigerant in the lithium bromide is about 35 ◦C. This is an inexpediently high
temperature diﬀerence (pinch), which could be improved somewhat to produce
more DC.
Figure 6.17 shows the improvements of the COP with increasing refrigerant
concentration. As ξr increases the absorber temperature drops to the point
where at ξr = 0.54 the temperature diﬀerence is less than 1 ◦C. The best suited
refrigerant concentration depends on the acceptable pinch temperature in terms
of economics. The lower the pinch, the larger the surface area is required in
the absorber to transfer the heat and the more expensive the component will
become.

6.9
Optimized design
The parametric study shows several possibilities for improving the system.
Both the SOFC temperature and utilization factor shows a clear local opti-
mum value in ﬁgure 6.3 and 6.5 respectively. Furthermore, improvements can
be done to the APP based on ﬁgure 6.17, indicating that the temperature
diﬀerence between absorbing temperature and cooling water being quite high.
Additionally, some factors display an approximately linear behaviour, such as
the SOFC stack number, turbo machinery eﬃciencies and pressure drops. Not
surprisingly, a higher isentropic eﬃciency will have a positive eﬀect on the sys-
tem performance, just as lower pressure losses will reduce pressure diﬀerences
in the system. These improvements are purely economical in nature, a higher
isentropic eﬃciency will in most cases result in a more expensive component.
To eﬃciently determine the best pumps and heat exchangers, the simulations
would have to include component prices and output values, something which
DNA does not support. Performing an economic based optimization of the
system is not within the scope of this thesis, as the entire system would have
to be re-designed in a less rigid simulation tool such as EES instead of DNA.
Two implementation scenarios has been conducted and compared to the base
case results. The ﬁrst (optimum1 case 1) includes only obvious improvements,
while optimum case 2 include more parameters.
Opt.
case 1 is the imple-
mentation a new SOFC operation temperature of 730 ◦C, a utilization factor
of 0.715 and a 5 ◦C temperature diﬀerence in the absorber by increasing the
refrigerant concentration to 50 %. Opt. case 2 uses Opt. case 1 improvements,
but additionally decreases prominent pressure losses and isentropic eﬃciencies
(for example by installed better quality equipment). The values can be seen in
appendix C table C.5.
Table 6.3 shows the results of Opt. case 1 and 2 simulations. Optimum case
1 shows a 1.17 percent point increase in thermal eﬃciency, while the exergetic
eﬃciency only increases with 0.85 percent point. This diﬀerence in eﬃciency
increases is due to the fact that when the top cycle performance is increased,
1It should be noted that this it not a true optimum, but rather a pseudo optimization,
as discussed in section 5.2.

the bottoming cycle decreases, which is conﬁrmed by the power and DE pro-

due to the lower auxiliary power consumption. The lower compressor work by
the air blower results in a lower air temperature out of the cathode preheater,
thus aﬀecting the bottoming cycle production of DE. These results are well in
line SOFC CHP theory, stating that as the top cycle improves the bottoming
cycle production will reduce, though possibly not at the same rate.
6.10
Summary
The exergetic eﬃciency does overall not make for a particular good parameters
in terms of optimization. Where the power production is involved, the param-
eter follows the thermal eﬃciency closely. For changes in parameters without
an impact on power production, the increase or decrease in exergetic eﬃciency
is so small, that they are hard to visualize. Had the heating or cooling been at
a higher or lower temperature respectively, the exergetic eﬃciency would have
been better correlated with the parameter changes. The exergetic eﬃciency is
likely a better performance parameter when comparing conceptually diﬀerent
systems. In hindsight, perhaps a diﬀerent reference state than the standard
had been more suitable.
The parametric studies show that not all parameters has a local optimum
within a reasonable interval of change. Some parameters display a linear or
near-linear behaviour. Increasing numbers of SOFC stacks or isentropic eﬃ-
ciencies will obviously always have a positive eﬀect on the total system, either
as higher production or lower auxiliary consumption of power and is only lim-
ited by what is economically viable.
For the compressors and pumps, the air blower has the largest impact on the
==PAGE:104==
88
CHAPTER 6. PARAMETRIC STUDY
system by a fair margin and should be the focus of the turbo machinery compo-
nents. The solution pump in the APP only has to supply the pressure diﬀerence
between high and low pressure components. In reality some component pres-
sure losses should be considered when selecting the solution pump.
The study shows that the gasiﬁcation temperature should not be increased
needlessly unless necessary. While the increased temperature beneﬁts the bot-
toming cycle production, it has a signiﬁcant impact on SOFC operation and the
exergetic eﬃciency in this case quite clearly shows that the trade-oﬀbetween
the two not being worth it. The temperature is likely strongly dependant on
the feedstock MSW composition and requires more in depth study and a more
advanced modelling approach. The performance of the gasiﬁer could likely be
improved to better represent a real gasiﬁcation reactor. The assumption that
both all carbon is converted and that no tars leave the reactor with the product
gas is likely unrealistic.
The optimized results show that some improvement of the system is possible,
between 1.07 and 2.34 percent points for the thermal eﬃciency depending on
the degree of improvements.
The ﬁrst optimization case only improves the
operational parameters within stated assumptions, while the second case ad-
ditionally increases isentropic eﬃciencies and decreases pressure losses. The
parametric study showed that the rich refrigeration concentration in the APP
could be improved, resulting in a higher COP.
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 7
Flue Gas Condensation
This chapter will brieﬂy investigate the beneﬁts of lowering ﬂue gas outlet
temperature and ﬂue gas condensation with regard to the bottoming cycle
district energy production. The base case from ﬁgure 5.7 and table 5.4 are
used for simulations.
7.1
Condensing ﬂue gas and 4th generation DH
Figure 5.3 in section 5.4 indicate that some water vapour is present in the ﬂue
gas. This vapour and the remaining sensible heat of the ﬂue gas represent a DE
production potential. As MSW in general contain high amounts of moisture, it
could be worthwhile to investigate if ﬂue gas condensation could improve the
system. One of the advantages of the system is the relatively clean ﬂue gas.
Most impurities are assumed to be removed by the gas cleaning system before
the SOFC chemical conversion and combined with the clean catalytic burner,
the ﬂue gas should be fairly clean and could be condensed. By condensing the
water in the ﬂue gas, the water heat of evaporation can be exploited and the
DE production increased.
Hebenstreit et al. conducted a study of ﬂue gas condensation for heat recovery
of biomass boilers. An increase between 3 and 21 % in primary energy eﬃciency
was found depending on the ﬂue gas outlet temperature. Temperatures as low
as 10 ◦C was utilized in the analysis. Hebenstreit conclude that for large scale
systems ≥10 MW ﬂue gas condensation is very economically viable, but also for
much smaller systems, in particular if the added heating production can replace
electric heaters[76]. Blumberga et al. carried out a similar study in 2011, with
ﬂue gas condensation of wood chip-fuelled boilers. The heat recovered in the
gas condenser ˙Qgc was calculated as:
˙Qgc = ˙Qc + ˙Qph
(7.1)
Where ˙Qc is the heat recovered by condensation and ˙Qph is the physical/sensible
heat of the ﬂue gas. The results shows that for a speciﬁc setup it was possible
to recover 100-180 kWh/MWh[77].
The 4th generation district heating will enable the heat rejection from the ab-
sorption plant condenser to be utilized, thus greatly increasing the performance
and economy of the absorption plant[6]. For small localized plants, where the
89
==PAGE:106==
90
CHAPTER 7. FLUE GAS CONDENSATION
energy transmissions distances are very small or basically non-existent (for ex-
ample if the plant is located in the basement of a large hotel/hospital), it is very
reasonable to assume that low temperature district heating could be viable. By
combining ﬂue gas condensing with 4th generation DH, the bottoming cycle
DE production can be improved signiﬁcantly.
7.1.1
Modelling of ﬂue gas condensation
Due to limitations of the desorber and heat exchanger components in DNA it is
not possible to directly cool down the ﬂue gas and beneﬁt from both the sensible
heat and the condensation of water vapour. Instead both a thermodynamic
model and a simulation model must be developed. The thermodynamic model
is the principle of the ﬂue gas condensation in the bottoming cycle, while the
DNA model is the necessary conﬁguration to facilitate the simulations of ﬂue
gas condensation. Since the temperatures of the district heating is signiﬁcantly
lower, the bottoming cycle conﬁguration is here revisited.
CB
Desorber
DH
T40 = 625◦C
T41 = 60◦C
T42 = 30◦C
T81 = 55◦C
T80 = 25◦C
T75 = 35.3◦C
T70 = 99.4◦C
Figure 7.1: Temperatures in the ﬂue gas condensation with 4th generation
DH for series connection bottoming cycle.
Figure 7.1 shows the thermodynamic model of ﬂue gas condensation in the
series connection. The ﬂue gas temperature at the outlet of the desorber must
be higher than the supply temperature of the district heating plus a reasonable
pinch temperature (here 5 ◦C). The same is the case out of the district heating
heat exchanger, the ﬂue gas temperature at the outlet of the DH heat exchanger
must be higher than the DH water return temperature, here using the same
pinch.
The parallel connection model can be seen on ﬁgure 7.2. The same principle
applies for the series connection, but the temperatures are diﬀerent. Since the
ﬂue gas ﬂow is split, the temperature out of the desorber can be lower than the
series connection (the refrigerant temperatures on the desorber side remains as
shown in the series connection).
The saturation temperature of water vapour in a ﬂue gas mixture of gasses can
be calculated via the partial pressure[69]:
pwater = xwater · pfluegas
(7.2)
Where the saturation temperature is evaluated in EES at increasing water

molar fractions on ﬁgure 7.3. As water condensates, the partial pressure drops,
lowering the condensation temperature.
In the base case the mole fraction of water is ≈0.098, resulting in a saturation
temperature of 46.6 ◦C, as seen by point A on the ﬁgure 7.3.
This means
that for the series connection, no ﬂue gas condensation of water takes place in
the desorber only the DH, which can be seen on ﬁgure 7.1. Point B on ﬁgure
7.3 shows the water mole fraction corresponding the outlet of the desorber
(T41) in the series conﬁguration. For the parallel connection on ﬁgure 7.2 the
temperature at the outlet of both the desorber and DH are below the saturation
temperature, so condensation takes place in both components.
7.1.2
DNA ﬂue gas condensation
As mentioned, the regular DNA components has some limitations with regard
to condensing gasses, the underlying mass and energy balances does not support
phase changes. A specialized component must be used instead, a Gascooler,
which can handle the liquid condensate as an output as well as the remaining
gasses.
The series connection with DNA node numbers can be seen on ﬁgure 7.4. Since
no condensation takes place in the desorber (the desorber outlet is higher than
the saturation temperature), the ﬂue gas can heat exchange directly in the
desorber, as is the case in the normal series conﬁguration. A gas cooler is im-
plemented instead of the regular DH heat exchanger. The 4th generation DH
is heated through the gas cooler in node 80 and 81, in the process cooling the
ﬂue gas and releasing heat from the water vapour in the ﬂue gas. The water
condensate leaves the gas cooler in the bottom of the component. This small
stream of water is not considered further, but could possibly have uses in the
system, either as cooling of components or in the gasiﬁcation process.
The parallel conﬁguration follows the same principle as the series, but here two
gas coolers are required, since the outlet temperature of the desorber and DH
heat exchanger are diﬀerent, as can be seen on ﬁgure 7.2. The parallel ﬂue gas
condensation DNA model is displayed on ﬁgure 7.5. The ﬂue gas is condensed
in the gas cooler and the heat is transferred to air, which can then be used to
transfer the heat to the desorber and DH heat exchanger. Air is used as no
phase change occur in the relevant temperature interval. This only occurs from

7.1.3
Veriﬁcation
To conﬁrm that the model is behaving as expected, the parallel ﬂue gas con-
densation model was compared to the base case parallel conﬁguration. Using
the ﬂue gas outlet temperature of 100 ◦C in both cases and matching the in-
let temperature of air and ﬂue gas, which is better seen in table 7.1, a direct
comparison can be made.
Recall that nodes 40 and 41 in the base case are the in- and outlet of ﬂue gas
in the desorber. In the new condensation model, nodes 40 and 41 are the in-
and outlet of the gas cooler, while 42 and 43 are the air inlet and out respec-
tively. The table clearly shows that for the same temperatures, the evaporator
production of DC is the same and the COP is maintained, showing that the
thermal input to the desorber is the same. From this it can be concluded that
at high temperatures, relative to condensation, the gas coolers behave as a
regular heat exchanger and that no discrepancies could be found and that the

7.1.4
Simulation results
Based on the assumption that the ﬂue gas condensation implementation in
DNA is working as expected, simulations are performed for the parallel and
series connection to determine which conﬁguration performs the best in terms
of performance and energy utilization.

7.2. SUMMARY
95
ﬂue gas condensation. The diﬀerences in performance are quite small in gen-
eral however and the conﬁgurations are inherently diﬀerent in terms of produc-
tion. The more determining factor is likely the demand proﬁle at the consumer
matching one or the other conﬁguration better. The overarching result here is
that with ﬂue gas condensation it is possible to better utilize the waste heat
from the SOFC, in particular when considering 4th generation DH as well,
enabling the heat rejected in the condenser to be utilized.
7.2
Summary
To summarize, the increase in district cooling production in the speciﬁc case
does not utilize the ﬂue gas condensation well.
From ﬁgure 7.3 it can be
seen that the amount of water in the ﬂue gas must be fairly high before the
temperature is high enough to utilize for the desorber in the absorption cycle.
The main beneﬁt from ﬂue gas condensation is only relevant if low temperature
4th generation district heating can be utilized in close proximity to the system.
In other systems with higher water content in the ﬂue gas the beneﬁt of ﬂue
gas condensation could be signiﬁcantly higher, in particular when connected to
low temperature processes such as the absorption plant.

Chapter 8
Exergy and Thermoeconomic
Analysis
This chapter evaluates the system from an exergetic and thermoeconomic per-
spective. Thermoeconomics1 combine exergy and economic analysis to help
determine the production cost of products in a thermal energy system[78]. As
previously mentioned, electricity is a homogeneous product, while heating and
cooling is not, therefore this chapter focuses on the production of power by the
SOFC.
The exergy analysis method was introduced in chapter 4 section 4.7.2. The ex-
ergy analysis can help determine which processes and components in a system
contribute the most to energy ineﬃciencies and losses, information which an
energy analysis can not help determine.
The exergy analysis and thermoeconomic calculates uses the optimized system
Opt. case 1 from chapter 6, section 6.9.
8.1
Thermoeconomic principle
The cost balance of a system can on general form be formulated as:
˙CP,tot = ˙CF,tot + ˙ZCI
tot + ˙ZOM
tot
(8.1)
Where ˙CP,tot is the cost rate of the system products, ˙CF,tot is the cost rate
of fuel inputs,
˙ZCI
tot is the cost rate of capital investment (CI) and ˙ZOM
tot
is
the cost rate related to operation and maintenance (OM) of the system (not
including fuels). The CI and OM are ﬁxed and gathered under the single term
˙Z, covering the annual contribution to the CI and OM over the system lifetime.
The rate is deﬁned as price per number of hours or seconds per year the system
in question operates and has the unit [$/kWh]. The cost rates ˙C are related to
the exergy streams both of power, heat and material.
In the following sections 8.2 and 8.3 the ﬁxed cost rate ˙Z is evaluated for each
component based on cost correlations and estimates of total capital investment
(TCI). Section 8.4 introduces the thermoeconomic and exergy balances required
to calculate ˙C and exergetic eﬃciencies for each component individually.
1Often called ”Exergoeconomics” as well.
97
==PAGE:114==
98
CHAPTER 8. EXERGY AND THERMOECONOMIC ANALYSIS
8.2
Component costs
The purchased equipment cost (PEC) correlations are for the most part based
on one or more dominant variables, which describes the size of the component.
For heat exchangers this is the heat transfer area, for a pump or compressor
the power consumption and so forth.
If nothing else is stated, the PEC is
based on [79] and excel spreadsheets provided by the supervisor. Since the
cost correlations are complied of several sources, there is lack of consistency
in input units. Furthermore, the cost correlations which can be obtained from
other literature are often viable for a small range of input values (which is not
always deﬁned). In particular components related to the SOFC are often based
on very small systems, in the 1 to 5 kW power production range and does not
scale to the 200 to 200 kW range in a reasonable and logical manner. Cost
correlations for components are in general notoriously diﬃcult to ﬁnd and a
signiﬁcant margin of error must be attributed to these estimates.
All prices are in U.S. dollar unless otherwise stated.
8.2.1
Solid oxide fuel cell
The SOFC cost are based on geometric factors and the fuel cell operating
temperature.
CSOF C = CSOF C,stack + CSOF C,aux
(8.2)
Where the cost of the stack is calculated as:
CSOF C,stack = Ncell · Acell · (2.96 · Tcell −1907)
(8.3)
The SOFC correlation is originally for a tubular SOFC design, but are assumed
to be viable for a planar SOFC as well. Garche et. al states that as of 2015
SOFC prices are about $7000 to $8000 per kW electrical production[80]. Ledón
et al. performs a detailed analysis of an high eﬃciency ethanol-fueled SOFC
in [81], here the SOFC cost of $1500/kW installed capacity was used. In [82]
Meyer et al. calculated a cost rate ˙Z of 432.0 e/h for the exergoeconomic
analysis (for comparison, the ˙ZSOFC calculated in this thesis is around 77 $/h).
Santin et al. states that there is much uncertainty about commercial SOFC
costs with regard to a SOFC-GT hybrid system, mentioning numbers in the
$400 to $800/kW range[83].
In general the SOFC component cost varies to the extreme in literature de-
pending on fuel, plant size, installed capacity or production and so forth. Fur-
thermore, it is not always obvious if the SOFC costs stated is for the component
alone or with auxiliary support systems as well. No general trend became clear
while investigating the costs and the correlation in eq. (8.3) is assumed to be
adequate for estimating the SOFC PEC.
The auxiliary cost for the SOFC is assumed to be 10 % of the SOFC stack
cost.
CSOF C,aux = 0.1 · CSOF C,stack
(8.4)
==PAGE:115==
8.2. COMPONENT COSTS

funding.

8.3
Cost of fuel
The cost of MSW as a fuel is not well deﬁned. Waste does not have a value per
the normal deﬁnition, but rather a cost of disposal. Instead the socio-economic
value of MSW as a fuel is used[88]. Based on a technical report by EA2 the
gain of importing waste is around 0.0144 $/kWh assuming a LHV of 13 MJ/kg.
8.4
Exergy and cost balance
General form of the thermoeconomic balance over component:
X ˙Cj,k,in + ˙Zk =
X ˙Cj,k,out
(8.20)
Where k is the k-th component in the system and j is the material or exergy
stream. The general form of the exergy balance is deﬁned in section 4.7.2.
By formulating a thermoeconomic balance for each component, a linear set of
2Energianalyse a/s.
==PAGE:119==
8.4. EXERGY AND COST BALANCE
103
equations for m exiting exergy streams can be solved iteratively in EES with
the help of m −1 auxiliary equations.
The auxiliary equations are supple-
ments to the thermoeconomic balances, related to the assumption of whether
an exiting exergy stream can be utilized further as fuel in another component
downstream[69].
The following sections shows the exergetic and thermoeconomic balances ex-
amples for diﬀerent component types.
8.4.1
MSW dryer
The dryer functions as a heat exchanger, with a cold and hot stream in- and
outlet. The dryer is special, as the waste fuel is introduced in this component.
MSW dryer
Steam inlet, 58
Steam outlet, 57
MSW outlet, 2
MSW inlet, 1
Figure 8.1: MSW steam dryer.
The exergy balance over the dryer:
˙E1 + ˙E58 = ˙E2 + ˙E57 + ˙ED + ˙EL
(8.21)
With the exergy losses being zero, as no streams or heat leaves the system, only
an exchange of exergy occurs. The corresponding thermoeconomic balance:
c1 ˙E1 + c58 ˙E58 + ˙ZDryer = c2 ˙E2 + c57 ˙E57
(8.22)
The cost of the MSW is c1 from section 8.3. The hot steam getting cooled has
the same value on both sides, as per the deﬁnition[69], meaning that c58 = c57.
8.4.2
Gasiﬁer
The gasiﬁer works as a reactor from an exergy and balance point of view. The
exergy balance over the gasiﬁer:
˙E2 + ˙E53 = ˙E3 + ˙E99 + ˙ED
(8.23)
With the ashes leaving the system:
˙E99 = ˙EL

The thermoeconomic cost balance being:

With the value of ashes assumed to be zero; c99 = 0. The ashes could depending

considered any further. The cost of c2 is found from the previous component,
the dryer.
8.4.3
Heat exchangers
Both the air heater, steam generator and cathode preheater are modelled as
a heat exchanger, following the same principle. Here the air heater (AH) is
shown as an example.

8.4.4
Compressors
For compressors and pumps an electrical input is included, which from the
theory is deﬁned as pure exergy. For the air blower the equations are shown
below.
Air blower

Figure 8.4: SOFC air blower.
The exergy balance:
˙E25 + ˙EW = ˙E26 + ˙ED + ˙EL
(8.28)
And the corresponding thermoeconomic balance:
c25 ˙E25 + cwEW + ˙ZAB = c26 ˙E26
(8.29)
Depending on the inlet an auxiliary equation might be required. If the inlet
stream is coming from another component, no other equation is required, but
if the inlet is air or water at ambient temperature, the cost is assumed to be
zero. For the air blower this is the case, as air is taken from the ambient, thus
c25 = 0. The cost of the work input cw is equal to the cost of power production
of the SOFC.
8.4.5
SOFC
The SOFC has two inputs and three outputs in the fuel and air together with
power production. The exergy balance over the SOFC:
˙E22 + ˙E27 = ˙E23 + ˙E28 + ˙EPSOFC + ˙ED + ˙EL
(8.30)
As there is no heat losses, the exergy losses are zero ˙EL = 0.
c22 ˙E22 + c27 ˙E27 + ˙ZSOF C = c23 ˙E23 + c28 ˙E28 + cw,SOFC ˙EPSOFC
(8.31)
The cost of the fuel (MSW gas) is calculated from the previous component, the
methanator. As the SOFC does not fully utilize the MSW product gas3, the
cost of the fuel at inlet and outlet is assumed to be the same, such that c22 =

c23. Furthermore, the cost of the oﬀair is assumed to be zero, c28 = 0. As the

Since the SOFC is the only component producing electricity, the production
cost can be found as the speciﬁc costs related to the power output cw,SOFC.
8.4.6
Other components
The thermoeconomic balance equations for the remaining components are not
listed.
The principle is very similar and the most interesting aspect is the
auxiliary equations. For the desulphurizer the sulphur is assumed to have no
cost for simplicity sake. In reality it is likely that this sulphur has some cost
associated with disposal, but could also be considered a valued product to
use in other processes. The methanator only has one input and output, so no
auxiliary equation is needed. The catalytic burner behaves as a regular burner,
which from the theory requires no additional equations[69]. The ﬂue gas and
steam splitters are considered part of the piping cost together with the steam
and air mixer, thus they have no separate cost rate ˙Z.
8.5
Evaluation
Based on the theory and cost calculations presented, the system performance is
evaluated to determine the cost of power production. Furthermore the exergetic
analysis results are presented and discussed.
8.5.1
Thermoeconomic results
With the cost rates ˙Z and cost balances evaluate, an EES script can be writ-
ten to calculate the cost of power production4. The thermoeconomic analysis
shows a SOFC power production cost of 0.5694 $/kWh (0.5 e/kWh or 3.8
DKK/kWh). Compared to other renewable or sustainable technologies, which
can be seen on ﬁgure 8.6, this is a quite high production price.

As already discussed brieﬂy in section 8.2.1, the PEC of the SOFC component

is even more pronounced. With continuing research and development of solid
oxide fuel cells the price should decrease even further, making the technology
able to better compete with more established power production methods.

the system if the gasiﬁer expenses are reduced.

8.5.2
Exergy analysis results
The exergy analysis is conducted with the theory presented in section 4.7.2.
The exergy fuel, product, destruction and losses are shown in table 8.4.
The table shows that only a two components in the top cycle have exergy losses,
the gasiﬁer and the desulphurizer. This is due to the assumption about no heat
losses in the components. The ash from the gasiﬁer and the sulphur from the
desulphurizer are the only material streams leaving the system. The ﬂue gas
leaving the catalytic burner could be considered an exergy loss.

Looking at the exergy eﬃciencies in table 8.5, it would at ﬁrst glance seem
like the dryer is the worst component in terms of exergy destruction. But the

% respectively. Meaning that while the dryer has a large degree of exergy de-
struction relative to the exergy product, both the fuel and product are quite
small in absolute values and relative to the system, the dryer ineﬃciencies are
insigniﬁcant.
