The components can be put in several overarching categories, chemical conver-
==PAGE:127==
8.6. SUMMARY
111
sion/reactions, mixing and heat transfer. Chemical reactions is a signiﬁcant
source of exergy destruction, due to the process irreversibility. The exergy de-
struction in a mixing process is related to the temperatures, such that mixing
two ﬂuids with a large temperature diﬀerence will yield a higher exergy destruc-
tion, compared to mixing at a small temperature diﬀerence. Several processes
can take place at the same time. Heat transfer follows the same principle as
mixing, the larger the temperature diﬀerences, the more exergy destruction
will occur.
Chemical conversion occurs in the gasiﬁer, SOFC and catalytic burner, inci-
dentally the three components with the highest YD. Of the three, the SOFC
accounts for over 41 % of the total exergy destruction. The chemical conver-
sions in the SOFC generates a large amount of heat which causes the exergy
destruction. For the gasiﬁer and catalytic burner, both mixing and chemical
conversion takes place resulting in a high exergy destruction.
The cathode preheater is another component with a high amount of exergy de-
struction. Here the temperature diﬀerence is over 750 ◦C between inlets of air
and oﬀ-air. The mass ﬂow in other heat exchangers is more moderate, making
them negligible compared to total the system destruction.
8.6
Summary
Based on available cost correlations, the SOFC and gasiﬁer PEC is very high
compared to the other equipment in the system. The age of some cost corre-
lations and lack of information causes signiﬁcant uncertainties regarding the
cost of the system in general.
The exergy analysis shows that the SOFC accounts for over 40 % of the ex-
ergy destruction in the system, due to the production of ”waste” heat. In the
catalytic burner and gasiﬁer both mixing and chemical reactions takes place,
making these components the other major exergy destruction contributors.
High temperature diﬀerences in heat exchangers should be avoided if possible,
as they cause a large amount of exergy destruction as well, in particular impor-
tant in the cathode preheater which has a high mass ﬂow. Due to assumptions
about heat losses, only a negligible amount of exergy is lost in form of ash and
sulphur leaving the system.
The cost of production for the optimized system is quite high compared to
other renewable/sustainable technologies. Whether the system should be able
to compete with other power producing systems is questionable due to the
added beneﬁt of MSW disposal and the reduction in both transport and trans-
mission losses.
The cost of power production is greatly dependent on the cost of the SOFC and
to some extent the gasiﬁer, while the fuel cost only has a small impact under
the given assumptions. Were the SOFC and gasiﬁer costs reduced, it is likely
that the impact of MSW fuel cost would increase correspondingly. The cost of
the gasiﬁer and SOFC is paramount for the economic viability of the system.
A study from 2013 on small scale biomass-gasiﬁer-SOFC systems reaches a
similar conclusion based on a thermoeconomic analysis[90].
==PAGE:128==
==PAGE:129==
Chapter 9
Discussion
The results of simulations has already been assessed and commented on indi-
vidually in the relevant chapters. Here follows a more general discussion of the
obtained results and related subjects relevant to the investigated system.
9.1
Design limitations
The system is designed and modelled in DNA, using a number of operational
assumptions. The gasiﬁer DNA model is originally veriﬁed for a downdraft
wood chip gasiﬁer[61], but possibly a ﬁxed bed gasiﬁer is not the best suited
for MSW gasiﬁcation. A ﬂuidized bed reactor could prove a better choice, in
which case the behaviour and performance of the model should be quite diﬀer-
ent. Furthermore, the gasiﬁer does not take chemical reactions into account,
but instead uses minimization of gibbs energy. As stated previously, the gasiﬁer
model used in this thesis does not mirror a speciﬁc reactor type, but rather the
gasiﬁcation process on a more general level. The eﬀect of these simpliﬁcations
is not easy to determine and the impact on system performance would require
a more detailed modelling approach.
In several ways DNA is a strong tool for design and simulation of thermal sys-
tems, but has some limitations with regard to available components as well.
The limitations are also seen with regard to the ﬂue gas condensation where
some additional components were needed to model the ﬂue gas water vapour
condensation.
A number of assumptions was necessary to design and model the system in
DNA and the parameters expected to be most signiﬁcant were studied in chap-
ter 6. This parametric study showed that many assumed values had an impact
on the thermal eﬃciency ηth, though most were within a ± 2 percent points
for a reasonable interval.
9.2
MSW as a fuel
Only certain types of waste have been investigated and thus not MSW in the
broadest deﬁnition. Information about household-like waste, which is a signif-
icant portion of incinerated waste, is more readily available compared to other
types, thus the analysis focuses on the areas where data seems applicable. Con-
113
==PAGE:130==
114
CHAPTER 9. DISCUSSION
struction waste for example is not well represented in this work and should be
further investigated if the system is operating in a setting where construction
waste constitutes any signiﬁcant fraction in the utilized fuel.
Some uncertainties are related to the waste characteristic method developed.
The material fractions are quite certainly not represented in equal measure in
the waste fractions as is assumed, which could lead to some deviations com-
pared to the actual values.
The fuel analysis shows that diﬀerent waste types have signiﬁcant impact on
the overall properties and performance of the system. Organic waste in par-
ticular is a bad fuel due to the high moisture content. It is possible that this
particular fraction of waste would be better utilized in a diﬀerent manner, for
example in a biomass digester to produce liquid fuels and compost. This would
obviously require more complex waste sorting and would decrease the amount
of MSW available to use as fuel in WtE power production. None the less an
interesting subject to investigate further.
9.3
System operation and performance
The investigated system has been simulated, yielding a thermal eﬃciency of 36
to 37.5 % depending on the quality of components. One explanation for the
”poor” eﬃciency compared to more standard SOFC systems is the fuel. MSW
has to be processed in the gasiﬁer before it can be fed to the SOFC and this
conversion requires energy, which is consumed directly from the fuel.
The temperature of the ﬂue gas enabled the system to function as a CCHP
with a bottoming cycle absorption plant and district heating connection. The
possibility of producing both heating and cooling greatly increases the areas
of application of the system, but requires a means of which to distribute this
district energy. The temperature of the ﬂue gas is more than adequate to pro-
duce both DC and DH which requires temperatures of at least 100 ◦C. Both
dual and triple stage absorption plants could be viable for additional cooling
production[36]. Other options of bottoming cycles have been investigated in
the past, for example with a Stirling engine[55] or a Organic Rankine Cycle
[91], both yielding a high thermal eﬃciency compared to the investigated sys-
tem.
The absorption plant operation is simpliﬁed somewhat in terms of modelling.
The heat rejection from the condenser and absorber is not designed and sim-
ulated in detail. The cooling water used in the absorber is assumed to always
be available in needed quantities, while the condenser heat is supposed to be
removed partly by hot water production and the rest by free cooling. For lo-
cations without available cooling water, a free cooler or cooling tower would
have to be used instead, thus increasing the auxiliary power consumption of
the system. For modelling the system in a speciﬁc location, the assumptions
about water cooling and free cooling in the absorber and condenser should be
questioned and investigated further. A possibility for improving the absorption
plant could be to introduce an internal heat exchanger to recover heat between
the desorber and absorber.
The ﬂue gas condensation did not oﬀer much beneﬁt with regard to the dis-
==PAGE:131==
9.4. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
115
trict cooling production, but was beneﬁcial for the district heating production
if 4th generation district heating was produced. It is not unlikely that by the
time the system will become more proﬁtable or sustainable from an economic
perspective, low temperature district heating could also be more widespread.
9.4
Economic analysis
The purchased equipment costs show that the SOFC and gasiﬁer components
are very expensive based on given assumptions and cost correlations. It is likely
that the utilized correlations are too out-dated. Furthermore the current time
value of the dollar has not been taken into account, which could have had an
impact on the expenses. For components still in development stage, such as
fuel cells and partly MSW gasiﬁers, component costs are not readily available
as is the case with more established technologies.
The thermoeconomic analysis found the power production cost to be fairly
high compared to other similar technologies. The analysis is based on numer-
ous assumptions about lifetime, yearly operation hours, maintenance factor,
inﬂation rate and so forth, all of which will have an impact. The PEC is ex-
pected to have the greatest eﬀect on the production price. Similar studies of
SOFC-gasiﬁcation integrated systems have found the same results. Moradin
et al. found the total investment cost of a wood-gasiﬁer-SOFC system to be
450’000 $ for a 60 kW rated power, somewhat comparable to the investigated
system considering the size diﬀerence[90].
9.5
Part load performance
The operation and control of the system in part load is not considered to any
extent in the thesis, but could potentially have a signiﬁcant impact on both
production and proﬁtability. The SOFC has some issues with load ﬂuctuations
and is best used for base load in an energy system[11].
This lines up well
with the fact that MSW has to be disposed of continuously, but does not
correspond well to regular consumer demand of power, heating and cooling. In
essence both part load operation and storage options should be considered as
well as simulation to evaluate performance in realistic situations. The storing
of heating and cooling in hot and cold water respectively is widely accepted,
while the storing of electricity has yet to be solved.
One interesting option is to operate the gasiﬁer continuously to produce gas
and store the gas for use when demand requires it. This could even be combined
with the reverse operation of the SOFC, using it as an electrolyte to produce
hydrogen from surplus renewable energy, with the added beneﬁt of keeping the
SOFC warm for easy switch to power production. There are both advantages
and disadvantages of storing the gasiﬁer product gas for later use. On one
hand adding a great deal of ﬂexibility to the system, but also to some degree
diminishing the beneﬁt of integration, as the sensible heat in the product gas
will likely be lost and additional preheating will be needed. This system is
presumably quite complex and must take demand proﬁles and waste generation
patterns into consideration on a detailed level to yield meaningful results.
==PAGE:132==
116
CHAPTER 9. DISCUSSION
9.6
Other considerations
The gasiﬁcation process allows MSW to be considered as a fuel for power
production with a SOFC. MSW is a ”free” fuel often either incinerated in
Scandinavia or simply landﬁlled in other parts of the world. In an energy eﬃ-
cient country like Denmark with a signiﬁcant amount of renewable energy, the
means of which to reduce emissions and pollution are limited. From a Dan-
ish and Scandinavian perspective the system is designed with energy recovery
from waste in mind, the primary function being to better utilize the waste as
a resource and improve the current waste handling scheme. For other parts
of the world the system could have a dual functionality, ﬁrst and foremost
the disposal of waste instead of landﬁlls and secondly as an energy recovery
process. Whether the system is viable compared to conventional WtE plants
in countries with more obvious ways of reducing emissions requires a diﬀerent
kind of analysis than conducted in this thesis.
In general the environmental impact is not considered at all in the thesis. This
could prove to be an interesting analysis, to compare emissions from conven-
tional WtE plants and from the investigated system. It is expected that the
SOFC and catalytic burner could greatly reduce the amount of CO2 emissions
per MWh produced compared to the technologies in use today.
==PAGE:133==
Chapter 10
Conclusion
A municipal waste gasiﬁer has been integrated with a solid oxide fuel cell and
an absorption plant to provide electricity, heat and cooling. Using the compo-
nent based simulation tool DNA, a thermodynamic model has been designed
and evaluated. The inclusion of a methanator has improved the design by low-
ering auxiliary power consumption and increasing bottoming cycle production
of district energy. The analysis shows that the thermal eﬃciency of the system
with methanator is 35.1 %, which could be enhanced to 36.1 % by modify-
ing assumed operational parameters and further to 37.4 % with higher quality
components. By improving the top cycle performance, the production of the
bottoming cycle is diminished, as less energy is available in the ﬂue gas. The
COP of the absorption plant is 0.7, a reasonably standard value according to
literature.
An exergetic eﬃciency of 32.8 % with methanator follows the thermal eﬃciency
closely, and does not bring much new information during the optimization pro-
cess. The exergy analysis however shows that most of the irreversible losses in
the system comes from the chemical conversions in key components and inex-
pedient temperature diﬀerences during heat exchange and mixing.
Using the developed method of determining MSW properties based on waste
fractions, several diﬀerent waste related fuels is investigated. The following
simulations shows that the MSW waste fractions and corresponding charac-
teristics have signiﬁcant impact on the system performance and should be a
key factor when determining the system viability at a speciﬁc location.
In
particular food scraps and vegetable waste proves to be a poor fuel due to the
embedded moisture.
During the thesis a small tool has been developed in Excel to easily calculate
the exergetic eﬃciency based on DNA output ﬁles. This tool could prove useful
for students to evaluate the performance of a thermodynamic system with few
user inputs.
From a thermodynamic point of view the investigated system results in a signif-
icant increase in performance compared to conventional waste-to-energy plants.
The thermoeconomic analysis shows that the cost of power production is ex-
ceedingly high compared to other sustainable technologies, primarily due to
the gasiﬁer and SOFC component costs. Analysis furthermore shows that if or
when the SOFC expenses decrease, the system can become viable and compete
with other technologies, despite the relatively poor fuel properties.

APPENDIX B. CALCULATIONS
B.1
Speciﬁc heat estimate
The speciﬁc heat (cp) of a solid can be estimated as:
cp,tot =
P
i
fi · cp,i
P
1
fi
(B.1)
Where fi is the fraction of each element and cp,i is the speciﬁc heat of each
element.
Hydrogen, oxygen, nitrogen and sulphur speciﬁc heats are evaluated from
EES[56], while the speciﬁc heat of carbon is based on graphite [93].
Table
B.1 shows the cp for each element at 300 K.
Table B.1: Speciﬁc heat of carbon, hydrogen, oxygen, nitrogen and sulphur
in kJ/kg.
Element

since EES does not support this element well).

B.2. PERFORMANCE PARAMETERS CALCULATION
143
B.2
Performance parameters calculation
Here follows the base case performance parameters presented in table 5.2 in
section 5.1.
The thermal eﬃciency is calculated as:
ηth =
Pnet
LHVwet · mMSW
(B.2)
With Pnet = 180.2247 kW, LHVwet · mMSW = 529.5169 kJ/s the thermal eﬃ-
ciency is:
ηth = 180.2247 kW
529.5169 kJ/s = 0.3404
(B.3)
For the simple exergetic eﬃciency:
ϵsys = Pnet + EDC + EDH
Efuel
(B.4)
Where EDC is calculated as the exergy increase in the DC chilled water:
EDC = E65 −E64 = 13.76 kJ/s −2.96 kJ/s = 10.8
kJ/s
(B.5)
And for the DH:
EDH = E81 −E80 = 2.47 kJ/s −1.71 kJ/s = 0.76
kJ/s
(B.6)
With the exergetic value of the fuel1 being 602.42 kJ/s the exergetic system
eﬃciency is:
ϵsys = 180.2247 kW + 10.8 kJ/s + 0.76 kJ/s
602.42 kJ/s
= 0.3184
(B.7)
The COP for the absorption plant as deﬁned in section 2.5 eq. (2.18).
COPh = Qe
Qg
= 212.1 kW
299.17 kW = 0.709
(B.8)
The pump power consumption is 1.175 · 10−3 kw or 1.175 W, having no impact
on the COP.
1In terms of value, but not conceptually being the same as the HHV times the fuel mass
ﬂow.
